From: plug-bounces at lists.pdxlinux.org

Subject: [PLUG] Bad-mouthing ext3

Several times I have complained here about ext3. It turns out that I may owe
ext3 an apology. 

The other day I was at PSU and my computer would not boot. The Compaq splash
screen came up, then nothing. Of course, this happened when I had a
presentation due in four hours and the Impress file was on the computer. And
I have to run it from the computer through the AV stuff in the classroom
because if I try to run an OOo file from my H drive it would not likely
work. I doubt that the university has OOo available on the computers in the
classroom, let alone the IPA fonts I needed or the sound clips I had links
to in the Impress file.

The help desk in the basement of Smith could not help, but suggested that
the CAT people in the CompSci building could probably assist a Linux user.
On the way to the CompSci building it dawned on me that if Grub was not
coming up, this must be a symptom that the hard drive was not found. You'd
think the BIOS would have the common sense to explain this to me instead of
just hanging, but we're talking cheap HP hardware here.

When I got to the CompSci building the first thing I asked for was a small
phillips head screwdriver so I could reseat the drive. Believe it or not, in
the entire place no one could come up with a small phillips head
screwdriver. 

Back to the basement of Smith, the help desk people ushered me into the back
room where students were busy putting electronic things together.
The jefe running the place asked a student to hand me a small phillips head
screwdriver, whereupon the student handed me a slot head screwdriver. There
ensued a discussion between the jefe and the student about the difference
between phillips head and slot head (I am not making this up), at the
conclusion of which I finally secured the loan of a small phillips head
screwdriver.

Two minutes later my computer was happily booting into Ubuntu.

The point is that the continual corruption I have been experiencing may have
nothing to do with ext3.

Jeme A Brelin wrote:
> 
> There are all kinds of interesting little specifications for each one 
> and I have a vague idea of what each of them would mean in terms of 
> performance.  However, if a person has an opinion on a drop-dead minimum 
> contrast ratio, I'd like to hear it.  I'm seeing that the difference in 
> price really jumps between 800:1 and 1000:1.  Am I really going to be 
> disappointed with the lesser contrast?

Unless things have changed, there isn't a standardized spec for 
measuring contrast.  Different manufacturers may not measure contrast
in the same way.  Hence contrast numbers should be taken with a huge 
grain of salt.

> 
> Is there another vital spec?  I'm assuming 6ms DVI is what I really want.

Because you are watching movies, a monitor with faster response may be 
desirable.  You probably want something with a sub 10 ms number.

galen

On Fri, 1 Dec 2006 07:21:35 -0800
"William A Morita" <wamorita at hevanet.com> dijo:

> What self respecting Linux user would not have a Knoppix or live Ubuntu copy
> handy to recover a file to a memory stick?
> At the very least, it would have shown you the issue with the hard drive
> immediately. 

Guess I'm not a self-respecting Linux user. But bear in mind that my
backpack already weighs nearly as much as I do. OK, that may be a
slight exaggeration. But in addition to the computer have to schlepp
half a dozen books, notebooks, computer peripherals, microphone,
headset, and even my lunch 20 km to PSU and back. Not sure a CD would
hold up very well in that environment.

Also, the first thing I did at the help desk in the basement of Smith
was to ask if anyone had a Knoppix or other live CD. The girl at the
counter went around to everyone asking if anyone had an "optics" CD.
(I'm taking Phonetics, I know what she said.) Anyway, upon discovering
what a Knoppix live CD was she suggested the CAT people in the 4th
Avenue building. It was on the way there that I realized that the
problem was probably the hard disk. 

Meantime I am working on a permanent solution. When I got home last
night I removed the hard disk to see if I might be able to fix it. I
was assuming that I had a faulty connector in the drive bay. I arrived
at this assumption because the problem occurred with two different hard
disks, the original and the new 80 GB 7200 rpm one that I replaced it
with. The culprit had to be either the connector in the drive bay or
the connector on the drive, and it would be unlikely to have bad drive
connectors with two brand new hard disks from different manufacturers.

But when I opened the drive bay and took a closer look I realized that
the hard disk connector was not meshing completely into the connector
in the drive bay. It was about 5 mm back from where it should have
been. The problem was that the screw holes in the sides of the drive
bay cover were 5 mm too far back. HP/Compaq should have made the holes
elliptical so I could slide the drive back and forth a bit, but the
holes are just plain round holes. I also noticed that the drive was not
fully down into the connector.

I solved the first problem by removing the screws and just leaving them
out. And I also placed a shim between the drive and the drive bay cover
to force it down more into the connector. So far my fix seems to be
working. If the drive slides around too much because of the missing
screws, then I'll get out the Dreml tool and fix the holes, or get out
the super glue or something. As a self-respecting Linux user I must be
prepared to get physical with my computer from time to time. Oh, and I
now pack a small screwdriver. :)

I just wanted to post that evidently there is nothing wrong with ext3.
Because I was having continual corruption I had assumed there must be
some glitch in ext3 when used on an amd-64 kernel or something. This
happened off and on from the first day after receiving the brand new
computer, and it seemed to me that a software problem was far more
likely than a brand new computer with component problems -- especially
as I know a couple of others with the same computer running Windows,
and they never had any problem.

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Fri Dec  1 15:05:15 2006
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Fri, 1 Dec 2006 15:05:15 -0800

On Thu, Nov 30, 2006 at 11:39:51PM -0800, Jeme A Brelin wrote:
> 
...
> So, I looked and found that there are some VERY affordable widescreen LCD 
> monitors that would be comparable to my current screen, but actually 
> readable and maybe radiating less and certainly taking up less desk space.
> 
> There are all kinds of interesting little specifications for each one and 
> I have a vague idea of what each of them would mean in terms of 
> performance.  However, if a person has an opinion on a drop-dead minimum 
> contrast ratio, I'd like to hear it.  I'm seeing that the difference in 
> price really jumps between 800:1 and 1000:1.  Am I really going to be 
> disappointed with the lesser contrast?
> 
> Is there another vital spec?  I'm assuming 6ms DVI is what I really want.

DVI solves one problem - if you use analog HD15, you may have problems
with sampling ripple with some old cards.  This makes a checkerboard
pixel array shimmer.  Not a problem with DVI, but folks that want to
use their old VGA port should be careful here.

Another thing that may matter is off-axis viewability.  How far off
"straight on" do you want an acceptable picture?  The stated "degrees"
are exaggerations;  the picture starts looking crappy long before the
big angles quoted.  One thing they do not quote is how it looks off
axis towards the corners;  the viewable angle on most monitors looks
like a blobby plus symbol, with crappy presentation for modest angles
towards the corners.  This is especially so with faster displays;
viewing angle trades off against LCD spped.

One thing that is important to us Portland area granola-heads is power
consumption.  The ViewSonic VG2021m that I bought (for $230 from "that
big store that hires the mentally challenged") is a bit of a watt hog
according to the comparative specs - 55W compared to typical 35W or
40W - but if I set the intensity to halfway and the power drops to
a tolerable 25W (this is measured with my Kill-A-Watt power meter,
available from GoLinuxShop for $18.13, see http://snurl.com/13uq0 ).
It is still quite bright.  I figure I will get extra life out of the
CCFL backlight, and have extra drive power in the future as the bulb
dims.

Oh, and one annoyance on the ViewSonic is the "Resolution Notice",
which pops up during boot to tell me that the monitor can do better
than 640x480.  Fortunately, you can turn it off.

Keith

From ehem at m5p.com  Fri Dec  1 20:12:46 2006
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Fri, 1 Dec 2006 20:12:46 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] LCD thingies
In-Reply-To: <20061201185519.8DD534183@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200612020412.kB24ClkB042325@m5p.com>

>From: Jeme A Brelin <jeme at brelin.net>
> So, I looked and found that there are some VERY affordable widescreen LCD 
> monitors that would be comparable to my current screen, but actually 
> readable and maybe radiating less and certainly taking up less desk space.

Watch out about radiating less, apparently 24" LCD electronics are close
to the power consumption of a 21" CRT.

> There are all kinds of interesting little specifications for each one and 
> I have a vague idea of what each of them would mean in terms of 
> performance.  However, if a person has an opinion on a drop-dead minimum 
> contrast ratio, I'd like to hear it.  I'm seeing that the difference in 
> price really jumps between 800:1 and 1000:1.  Am I really going to be 
> disappointed with the lesser contrast?
> 
> Is there another vital spec?  I'm assuming 6ms DVI is what I really want.

Ideally, sure. Note that the often measure the transition time starting
from grey, so the real response time is higher. There are two really big
gotchas that you'll want to consider:

LCD color reproduction isn't anywhere near as good as CRT color
reproduction. This is most significant with black, LCD black doesn't tend
to be very dark at all. They also tend to quote contrast ratios with
brightness at maximum, so black is even less black, just the whites are
even brighter. This may or may not be acceptable to you.

LCDs have a fixed, and often rather low resolution. Most 19" LCDs are
1280x1024 at best, while 19" CRTs will do 1600x1200 or better. Even
though you get more surface area, that surface area may be of limited
value due to fewer pixels. This may be a major factor behind multiple
monitor setups becomine common, you're compensating for a device that
doesn't allow high resolution.

Intertwined with the last point, sure widescreen might be useful, but
non-widescreen may be better value. A 21" 4:3 display is significantly
larger than a 21" 16:9 display. Particularly there is a large chance
you'll get quite a few more pixels on a 4:3 display than a 16:9 display.
At which point, at times when you want 16:9, simply allow the LCD to
letterbox, and you may still get the same resolution out of the 4:3
display.

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Fri Dec  1 22:41:40 2006
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Fri, 1 Dec 2006 22:41:40 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Kill-a-Watt (was LCD thingies)
In-Reply-To: <4570E179.8060805@dslnorthwest.net>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0611302335320.2617@shaft.bitmine.net>
	<20061201230514.GA20352@gate.kl-ic.com>
	<4570E179.8060805@dslnorthwest.net>
Message-ID: <20061202064140.GA24319@gate.kl-ic.com>


Keith Lofstrom wrote:
>(this is measured with my Kill-A-Watt power meter,
>available from GoLinuxShop for $18.13, see http://snurl.com/13uq0 ).

Denis Heidtmann wrote:
> What is the maximum current that meter will handle?

Maximum is 15A, 125V.  It only measures down to 0.01 Amps, so low
power devices like wall warts don't register accurately, but it
seems to be accurate within a watt or so.  It can measure VA 
(including reactive currents), and measure accumulated kilowatt
hours as well.  I was surprised to find that our refrigerator 
averages only 47 watts over the course of a day, and that the 
Thinkpad AC adapters I leave scattered all over do not register
even 1 watt when not driving a laptop.

BTW, I use an instrument called a "curve tracer" to look at
the AC adapters in more detail - they consume 5mA of "reactive
current", and no measurable "resistive current" when running
unloaded, which means that they only cause a milliwatt or two
of extra generator load to the power company - power line heating
losses out to the substation where the phase correctors are - but
nothing like the 1 or 2 watts that an unloaded wall wart consumes.  

It is educational to measure the power consumption of devices
in the office and the house.  The biggest current draw in my
computer lab is the Tektronix Phaser 350 color "crayon" printer.
Our largest "electrical sin" for our house is our water heater,
due to my daily hot soaky baths in the evening.  I will have to
work on that ...  but not now, it is time for my bath :-)

So buy some power meters, and give them as Christmas gifts.  Your
soon-to-be-ex friends who should use less electricity will be
reminded of your strident hectoring even when you are not there!

Keith

Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Sun Dec  3 12:38:10 2006
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Sun, 3 Dec 2006 12:38:10 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Shuttle ST62K fan failure
Message-ID: <20061203203810.GA4294@gate.kl-ic.com>


A year ago, I bought a Shuttle ST62K small format PC for my wife's
office, and it has been running SUSE 10.0 and automatically updating
and doing nightly backups back home to the server.  All well and good.

However, the fan has a problem, and from a google search I've learned
that many people have the same problem.  Either the fan device itself,
or the power controller on the CPU board, have problems, and I suspect
both.  About 6 months ago, the fan stopped running, so that the CPU
heated up to >100C and then shut down the system.  Moving the fan plug
from "FAN1" to "FAN3" cured that.  A week ago, the overheating started
occuring again.  The fan will come on under some circumstances, but
mostly not, and the machine never stays running for long.

The fan will run directly off the 12V supply, but it is very loud
and cools way more than necessary.  For normal operation on the 
ST62K, running the fan between 6 to 8 volts keeps everything properly
cooled, and the fan is much quieter.

The expedient hack suggested by some folks on the web is to connect
the fan from the 12V to the 5V connection on one of the 4 pin disk
power supplies, hard wired, bypassing the speed control and powering
the fan with 7V = 12V-5V.  The problem with this hack is that electric
motors without speed controllers look like dead shorts until they are
turning, so that at system startup the motor could drag the 5V supply
rail up to the 12V supply.  This would also occur if for some reason
the fan jammed or was shorted.  Bye-bye peripherals running on 5V. 

The hack I came up with was a little more work - I powered the fan off
the 12V supply to ground, but dropped the voltage with 8 power diodes,
which lowered the voltage to around 7V.  One advantage of the diodes
is they have a "negative temperature coefficient", which means the 
voltage across them drops a bit when they get hotter, and that means
a little more voltage for the fan when the system heats up.  I have
the diode strings running in front of the airflow to the fan (surrounded
by heat shrink tubing).  A major hack, but it keeps the system cool.
Perhaps too well, the CPU temperature is at 38C right now and it could
stand to be higher, while I imagine the system sounds pretty loud in
my wife's office on a quiet weekend.  I may add some more diodes.

BTW, I could not quickly find a gnome panel applet that works with
SUSE 10 that displays cpu temperature.  That would help my wife keep
tabs on the machine.  What I do instead is run a little script:

 #!/bin/bash
 while true
 do
 date | perl -pe 's/\n/ /'
 acpi -t
 sleep 60
 done

That prints the temperature every minute, into a two line text window.
I run the same script from my home office over the vpn, so I can watch
this kludge for a few days.

Keith

Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993

On Sat, Dec 02, 2006 at 03:43:48PM -0800, Russell Senior wrote:
> 100 dpi, but the subtended angle of each pixel can be calibrated by
> adjusting how far away you sit.  No distortion in the corners.  Black
> looks black.  White looks white.  It plenty bright for me, kimosabe.

When comparing LCDs and monitors, one should also consider power,
radiation, deskspace consumed, pollution, and fire hazard.  A friend's
house was burned down by flames from a failing monitor high-voltage
circuit.  Fire can also happen with the high voltage that drives the
CCFL backlight in an LCD (and oh how I wish they would use ultrabright
white LEDs instead), but it does use quite a bit less power and voltage,
so the fire hazard is less, all other things equal.

Analog monitors contain a few pounds of lead-glass to cut down the
X-ray radiation, so if they are improperly discarded they will pollute.
And the cathodes of the CRTs will wear out.  So will the CCFL, but
that is much cheaper to replace (though an extreme pain in the butt
for laptops, I just did so, poorly).

A year ago, I was working on a project that would have blown away
both technologies had it worked.  It was an ultra-low-cost projection
system that magnifies and optically amplifies and color-splits the
monochrome image off a single very small LCOS display chip and projects
a large color image.  40 inch projection display for $300, anyone?  Yes,
with folded optics and plastic mirrors it would still occupy about half
the thickness of an analog CRT, but it would have been quite light and
very low power because it used all the photons out of a low cost lamp.
Sadly, there were too many pixel defects in the optical amplifier, and
fixing them would have taken too much basic research.  But someday,
someone will find another kind of optical amplifier with fewer defects,
and all CRTs and most LCD systems will get replaced by it. 

No more CRTs in landfills.  Sigh.

Until then, reycled CRTs (until they die) are the best, with LCDs
being the best for new purchases.

Keith

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Sun Dec  3 16:27:00 2006
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Sun, 3 Dec 2006 16:27:00 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] LCD thingies
In-Reply-To: <45734003.5030400@verizon.net>

Keith Lofstrom wrote:
>
>No more CRTs in landfills.  Sigh.
>
>Until then, reycled CRTs (until they die) are the best, with LCDs
>being the best for new purchases.


On Sun, Dec 03, 2006 at 01:22:11PM -0800, Winterbeast wrote:
>       Mr. Lofstrom, 
>             I have a question for you. What happens when the LCD's 
> start hitting the landfills?
>    Sure, there isn't as much glass and phosphor, but are there other 
> things that may be worse?

Keith writes:

No.  If they are made with lead free solder, you could pretty much
grind one up into powder and eat it.  Well, I exaggerate a bit, the
electronics may contain a small amount of heavy metal, like tantalum
in some of the capacitors, but these are milligram amounts rather than
kilograms as in CRTs.  Most of the weight of an LCD monitor itself is
plastic and glass, and those are very low toxicity (non-leaded glass
is just modified sand, mostly).  The electronics industry is cleaning
up its act rapidly, and very much concerned about durability and
recyclability, because there are enough concerned customers to make
the extra effort pay off.  Look for "RoHS" labelling (Restriction of
Hazardous Substances, an EU program) on new products;  this new program
has some reliability concerns, but they should be worked out in time.

The actual LCD goop itself is a very thin layer - a micron or so -
between two sheets of conductor-coated glass.  The goop is usually
some exotic CNOH organic, probably biodegradable in time.  So a 20
inch panel might have a quarter cubic centimeter of goop.  You burn
off more automobile tire getting the thing home from the store.

The question becomes, why would LCDs hit the landfills?  There is
very little to wear out, besides the cold cathode fluorescent
backlight, which can be replaced.  Of course, all electronics can
wear out, but many of the high stress components are replacable if
a repair industry exists.  One reason LCDs would hit the landfills
is obsolescence, but if they are still useful, there are places like
Free Geek that can find new homes them.  Some might get accidentally
smashed screens, but the other parts are still usable as spares. 
What remains is bad design, or evil design --- LCDs with mis-features
or poor reliability that make nobody want them.

What remains is the significant labor, materials, power and water
that go into making LCDs, or any other manufactured item for that
matter.  So don't buy more than you need, buy well-made goods,  and
treat what you do buy with the same care that went into making it,
then get it to a responsible recycler (like Free Geek) when you 
stop needing it.

Keith

Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993

Keith Lofstrom <keithl at kl-ic.com> dijo:

> plastic and glass, and those are very low toxicity (non-leaded glass
> is just modified sand, mostly).  

At the risk of going off-topic, this discussion has me wondering a bit
about lead and glass. Meine elders were Deutsche and I have inherited
the odd "lead crystal" object. Now, it occurs to me that, in the
unlikely event such items made their way into a landfill, the lead
would be pretty much trapped inside the glass. Not that I am going to
drink high acid wine from them, but do we really need to worry about
lead inside of glass? Yes, I suppose given enough time the lead would
leach out. But I'm guessing we're talking about tens of thousands of
years here. Surely Ma Nature can handle that, right? For that matter, I
can go to any of a number of stores right here in PDX and buy more lead
crystal. Were it really that evil, wouldn't it be banned by now?

So why are we so worried about lead in the glass in CRTs? Or is this
just one of those pop culture things where people hear the word "lead"
and freak out without thinking things through?

I bad-mouth ext3 all the time.  Probably unfounded as I'm no filesystem
expert, but I've been using ReiserFS since 2.2.19 (custom woody install).

on a laptop and occasionally it gets turned off without shutting down
properly.

with jfs, I have to switch to a console and jfs_fsck and all is fine.
Reiser never had these issues.

journaling now.

> On Sun, 3 Dec 2006, Keith Lofstrom wrote:

>  #!/bin/bash

>  do

>  acpi -t
>  sleep 60
>  done

On Mon, Dec 04, 2006 at 10:15:18AM -0800, Paul Heinlein wrote:
> If you don't want to fire up perl every 60 seconds, you can accomplish 
> the date line using nothing but shell builtins:
> 
>   echo -n $(date)' '

Good idea, thanks for teaching me something.  I can also combine both
commands:   echo $(date) $(acpi -t) ... and that makes the script a
little cleaner looking as well as more efficient.  So it now becomes:

  #!/bin/bash
  while true
  do
  echo $(date) $(acpi -t)
  sleep 60
  done

Oh well, I'm still learning to use Perl, and only slowly learning
to /not/ use Perl when it is unnecessary.  :-)   I could do the
whole thing in Perl rather than bash, using the date and sleep 
functions and calling acpi with a system call, but I will leave 
that to Randal as a one-line exercise!  :-)

Date: Mon, 4 Dec 2006 11:58:26 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] LCD thingies (lead glass)
In-Reply-To: <20061203172622.560c7de8.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <200612022311.kB2NB72T047714@m5p.com>
	<8664ctzwwb.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
	<20061203210839.GB4294@gate.kl-ic.com>
	<45734003.5030400@verizon.net>
	<20061204002700.GA5874@gate.kl-ic.com>
	<20061203172622.560c7de8.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <20061204195826.GC18109@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Sun, 3 Dec 2006 16:27:00 -0800
Keith Lofstrom <keithl at kl-ic.com> dijo:

> plastic and glass, and those are very low toxicity (non-leaded glass
> is just modified sand, mostly).  
 
On Sun, Dec 03, 2006 at 05:26:22PM -0800, John Jason Jordan wrote:
> At the risk of going off-topic, this discussion has me wondering a bit
> about lead and glass. Meine elders were Deutsche and I have inherited
> the odd "lead crystal" object. Now, it occurs to me that, in the
> unlikely event such items made their way into a landfill, the lead
> would be pretty much trapped inside the glass. Not that I am going to
> drink high acid wine from them, but do we really need to worry about
> lead inside of glass? Yes, I suppose given enough time the lead would
> leach out. But I'm guessing we're talking about tens of thousands of
> years here. Surely Ma Nature can handle that, right? For that matter, I
> can go to any of a number of stores right here in PDX and buy more lead
> crystal. Were it really that evil, wouldn't it be banned by now?
> 
> So why are we so worried about lead in the glass in CRTs? Or is this
> just one of those pop culture things where people hear the word "lead"
> and freak out without thinking things through?

It is a matter of quantity and disposal rates, I think.  There are 
many pounds of lead glass in a CRT to cut down X-rays, at the maximum
possible lead concentration; sometimes 5 pounds of lead in a large CRT.  
The CRT works for about 5 years, then off to the landfill it goes.
OTOH, your inherited lead crystal objects have much less lead by
weight in them, and are kept for a long time, as your example shows
(I have some similar 80yo objects).  So the rate that the material
enters the landfill is far lower.

As far as how fast it leaches out, that partly depends on how thick
it is (diffusion time is proportional to the square of the thickness)
and how small the fragments are.  But it does come out, quite a bit
faster than thousands of years, and it can be somewhat nasty if some
ends up as airborne particles and somebody is nearby to breath it in.

I agree that the fretting probably does more damage than the lead
does, but it is wasteful to make a mess when you don't need to. 
So I look forward to the day when we make CRTs go away, and also the
day when people find something better to do than worry uselessly.

Keith

Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993

                                 PRESENTATION

                            Ingres is Open Source

                                      by

                                 John Murphy
                           <john.murphy at ingres.com>
                                     and
                                    others

      Ingres is a well known Relational Data Base Management
      System similar to Oracle, DB2, and many others.
      Like Oracle, Ingres has been around for a long time,
      has a significant user base and I (David Mandel) believe
      it has more built-in and/or add-on tools than postgres or
      mySQL.

      Ingres has been available on Linux for quite some time,
      but Ingres is now Open Source and Ingres Corporation
      is developing an Open Source strategy for Ingres.

      John Murphy will be in town and will tell us more about Ingres.
      He is also hoping to bring an associate to discuss a new
      generation of Ingres where they bundle a Linux distribution
      from rPath as part of Ingres.

From sean.whitney at gmail.com  Mon Dec  4 14:18:49 2006
From: sean.whitney at gmail.com (Sean Whitney)
Date: Mon, 04 Dec 2006 14:18:49 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] new laptop
In-Reply-To: <45749720.3030007@verizon.net>
References: <45749720.3030007@verizon.net>
Message-ID: <1165270729.4352.22.camel@einstein.seansdomain.org>

I recently bought a HTPC that has a 6150 nvidia chip in it.  I spent
some time trying to get it to work under ubuntu, but the driver needed
was in feisty fawn not edgy.  I spent some time with a ubuntu install
somewhere between edgy and feisty before I finally scrapped it all
together and installed debian.  Packages are too closely tied to each
ubuntu release to allow for that kind of installation.

Adding
#Nvidia files
#use this source line for unstable drivers

deb http://people.debian.org/~rdonald/nvidia unstable
nvidia-graphics-drivers
deb http://people.debian.org/~rdonald/nvidia unstable
nvidia-kernel-common
deb http://people.debian.org/~rdonald/nvidia unstable nvidia-settings
deb http://people.debian.org/~rdonald/nvidia unstable nvidia-xconfig
deb http://people.debian.org/~rdonald/nvidia unstable modules

to my /etc/apt/sources.list file installed all the drivers I needed.


Sean

> didn't do, please?

I have a similar laptop.  I don't use Ubintu though.

Here is what I do to get it to work under Fedora.

Download the latest NVIDIA commerical driver.

Make sure the kernel-devel and xorg-sdk is installed.

Boot into runlevel 3.

Run the install package.  Make sure X has the current config for the new 
drivers.

Start x by hand and see if it works.

If you are using Compiz/Beryl there are some extra options that need to be 
set in the config file.  Email me and I can dig out the list.

Breezy, and I did so on the R3000 wiki:

http://prinsig.se/weekee/index.php/Main_Page

But I didn't write anything additional about it after going to Dapper

didn't write up anything on the NVidia driver because it went without
problems for me -- the instructions I found on the Ubuntu 64-bit forum
worked, so why repeat what was already written?

on the Ubuntu 64-bit forum. You can search there for posts under my
name there:

http://ubuntuforums.org/forumdisplay.php?f=134

Not that I have found a lot of solutions myself. But the threads often
contain fixes to my problems that others have contributed.

Note that I am not currently using the NVidia drivers. The only reason
I installed them to begin with was because I couldn't get movies to
play with the open source driver. But with Dapper the open source
driver has no problems with movies, so I just left well enough alone.

Are you looking for 3D or 2D?  Open Source or free/cheap?

qCad will already be in most distributions.

Varicad might do the trick if you need 3D/parametric and want to pay a 
little.

BRLCad is great if you're making tanks and simulating ballistics, but 
not exactly approachable (at least not last time I looked at the gui, 
but there's been talk of improving that.)

Joe, Bob, Jon, Sue, and TimCAD are usually not done or good.

Don't even look at LinuxCAD.  Run away.

The short answer is that we're not there yet.  Want to write some code?

CAD systems. I'm using PythonCAD as a result. It's fairly simple and far

end a line insertion produced a dimension line with arrow heads and the
length in the center.

will use Art of Illusion as the source of STL files, and so I'm starting
to look at Art of Illusion.

RepRap: http://reprap.org/

Art of Illusion: http://www.artofillusion.org/

>

>In Oregon?  Yeah, that is going to work well...

I was recently at a childrens birthday party where the house had been
"greened".

It had a very low profile solar array, as well as a solar hot water
pre-heater.

This pre-heater fed sun warmed water into a tank-less natural gas water
heater.

It was quite the setup, but the temperature of water going into the water
tankless was 120 degrees.

Basically, in the summer you use no gas to heat your water.  I was told that
even in the winter

Temeratures of 70 to 80 degrees input are normal.  The solar array produced
a full 1/3 of the homes

Total electric bill.  

I'd say that's at the very least a great step in the right direction.

-Bill Ensley
Bear Printing

Remember a lot of time's solar is putting energy back into the grid....
Or reducing what you are consuming from the grid.  Solar in Oregon makes
a lot of sense, during the summer we have very long days with a lot of
sunshine.  That helps reduce the load on the grid that SoCal is putting
on it in the summer running air conditioning.  In the winter not so
much, solar here, but SoCal has lots of solar to add back to the grid to
help heat Keith's bathwater.

On Tue, 5 Dec 2006, Carla Schroder wrote:
> On Tuesday 05 December 2006 14:10, Jeme A Brelin wrote:
>> On Tue, 5 Dec 2006, Carla Schroder wrote:
>>> O west-side-centric persons, the larger portion of the state is sunny
>>> and dry.
>>> signed, living on the right side
>>
>> ...where you have to ship in loads of energy and water or else live on
>> shrubs and berries.
>
> Huh? I'm guessing that's supposed to be funny.

Not at all.

> But I don't get it.

Of course you don't.

> Nobody ships in water, except the silly people who pay money for bottled 
> tap water, and I'll wager Portland stores get their stocks of bottled 
> water from just as far away. Quite a bit of energy is generated on the 
> east side of the state: solar, wind, geothermal, and cogeneration. A 
> fair number of folks live completely off the grid. Soo.. did you have a 
> point in there somewheres?

It's about the agriculture.  In order to sustain people out there, 
agriculture must be heavily industrialized -- large-scale irrigation, 
industrial fertilizers, and oil, oil, oil.

Also, the people are further spread out, so distribution of goods and 
services is done almost entirely on a per-household basis by private 
automobile over relatively large distances.

Constrast this to the potentials of living in a well-populated, fertile 
valley and the responsibility factor just skyrockets (not to mention the 
cultural opportunities and means for social growth and enrichment).

J.

      Jeme A Brelin
     jeme at brelin.net

   I run only SSH2; version 1 is disabled. In the config files I specify that
pass phrases are to be requested first, and passwords only if [Enter] is
pressed at the first request. This used to work.

   It stopped working a long time ago and my sporatic research has not found
a reason. I've even sent e-mail to the supposed security gurus who write for
linux publications, but have not received a reply.

   Locally, using only a password is fine; we're behind the firewall and
communications is among local hosts. But, when I'm away from the office I'd
feel more comfortable typing my passphrase for access, especially on a
wireless public network (or, public wireless network).

   If you folks who deal with security have suggestions on how to debug this
situation, please let me know.

TIA,

Rich

From teknotus at gmail.com  Tue Dec  5 18:56:59 2006
From: teknotus at gmail.com (Daniel Johnson)
Date: Tue, 5 Dec 2006 18:56:59 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] SSH Experts Here?
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0612051739570.30253@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On 12/5/06, Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> wrote:
>    I run only SSH2; version 1 is disabled. In the config files I specify that
> pass phrases are to be requested first, and passwords only if [Enter] is
> pressed at the first request. This used to work.
>
>    It stopped working a long time ago and my sporatic research has not found
> a reason. I've even sent e-mail to the supposed security gurus who write for
> linux publications, but have not received a reply.
>
>    Locally, using only a password is fine; we're behind the firewall and
> communications is among local hosts. But, when I'm away from the office I'd
> feel more comfortable typing my passphrase for access, especially on a
> wireless public network (or, public wireless network).
>
>    If you folks who deal with security have suggestions on how to debug this
> situation, please let me know.
>
> TIA,
>
> Rich

Hi Rich,

I find that usually problems using keys instead of passwords is a
permissions issue.  If anyone, or any group other than you, or root
has read or write access to the private key, or to the directory above
it, or if it has write access to the authorized_keys2 file it won't
work.  There is usually some kind of helpful message as to why ssh
didn't work as expected in /var/log/secure, especially if you up the
log level.
-- 
Dan

From ehem at m5p.com  Tue Dec  5 19:17:51 2006
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Tue, 5 Dec 2006 19:17:51 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] SSH Experts Here?
In-Reply-To: <20061206030508.A668A42DC@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200612060317.kB63Hqgk078138@m5p.com>

>From: Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com>
>    Locally, using only a password is fine; we're behind the firewall and
> communications is among local hosts. But, when I'm away from the office I'd
> feel more comfortable typing my passphrase for access, especially on a
> wireless public network (or, public wireless network).

As long as you check host keys, there isn't really any practical
difference. Your target machine will have the password, if it is
compromised, an attacker *will* get your password (when you log onto the
console if nothing else). Pass-phrases are really most important when
you're going unencrypted through somewhere, either an intermediate sshd
host, or through wireless without encryption.

>    If you folks who deal with security have suggestions on how to debug this
> situation, please let me know.

The logs have already been suggested. `ssh -v` is a tremendous source of
information, in the worst case stopping `sshd` and then running `sshd -d`
is another source of information (remember to fully restart sshd when
done!).

From ehem at m5p.com  Tue Dec  5 19:56:27 2006
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)

Subject: [PLUG] LCD thingies
In-Reply-To: <20061203200005.962824194@ulke.nakedape.cc>

>From: alan <alan at clueserver.org>
> My laptop is 1920x1200.  They seemed to have stopped selling that 
> resolution though. I think it made the mouse cursor just a bit too small.

Precisely. If you can't get it, what is the point of saying it exists?
Yet scaling everything would of taken care of this.  :-(   I suppose that
will be nVIDIA's next mission, pursuading MS to make the items under
"accessibility options" much more prominent and encouraging LCD makers to
go super high resolution (more pixels means more graphics horsepower is
needed).


>From: Keith Lofstrom <keithl at kl-ic.com>
> CCFL backlight in an LCD (and oh how I wish they would use ultrabright
> white LEDs instead), but it does use quite a bit less power and voltage,
> so the fire hazard is less, all other things equal.

Those are coming, some LCDs are reported to already use them. What looks
better to me would be a direct matrix of RGB LEDs. With the LEDs being a
directly emissive medium, you get the colors back. Doesn't solve the
issue of ridiculously low resolution matricies though.


>From: Keith Lofstrom <keithl at kl-ic.com>
> The question becomes, why would LCDs hit the landfills?  There is
> very little to wear out, besides the cold cathode fluorescent
> backlight, which can be replaced.  Of course, all electronics can
> wear out, but many of the high stress components are replacable if
> a repair industry exists.  One reason LCDs would hit the landfills
> is obsolescence, but if they are still useful, there are places like
> Free Geek that can find new homes them.  Some might get accidentally
> smashed screens, but the other parts are still usable as spares. 
> What remains is bad design, or evil design --- LCDs with mis-features
> or poor reliability that make nobody want them.

Given how awful even the LCDs being sold today (resolution and color!) I
imagine obsolescence will be a major issues with LCDs for a while yet.
Meanwhile, though very heavy (and requiring proper disposal) CRTs are a
mature technology.


>From: Keith Lofstrom <keithl at kl-ic.com>
> The CRT works for about 5 years, then off to the landfill it goes.
> OTOH, your inherited lead crystal objects have much less lead by
> weight in them, and are kept for a long time, as your example shows
> (I have some similar 80yo objects).  So the rate that the material
> enters the landfill is far lower.

Which 5 years would that be?

It has been quite some time since I last saw a CRT die. I've only been
seeing them dumped due to replacement with lighter LCDs, I pretty well
haven't been seeing them /die/.

CRTs weren't being repaired for the same reasons as LCDs, it is expensive
to do so and the price of new ones was low. At the moment CRT prices are
still depressed since folks are still replacing them with LCDs. I'm
wondering if CRT prices will spring back and repair shops will become
profitable again due to the dump phase ending...

> I agree that the fretting probably does more damage than the lead
> does, but it is wasteful to make a mess when you don't need to. 
> So I look forward to the day when we make CRTs go away, and also the
> day when people find something better to do than worry uselessly.

Unlikely to happen. Airplane scares still get the front page when you're
massively more likely to be killed in a car wreck...

From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Tue Dec  5 22:18:44 2006
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Tue, 05 Dec 2006 22:18:44 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] SSH Experts Here?
In-Reply-To: <200612060317.kB63Hqgk078138@m5p.com>
References: <200612060317.kB63Hqgk078138@m5p.com>
Message-ID: <1165385924.31961.5.camel@spartacus.nakedape.priv>

On Tue, 2006-12-05 at 19:17 -0800, Elliott Mitchell wrote:

> As long as you check host keys, there isn't really any practical
> difference. Your target machine will have the password, if it is
> compromised, an attacker *will* get your password (when you log onto the
> console if nothing else). Pass-phrases are really most important when
> you're going unencrypted through somewhere, either an intermediate sshd
> host, or through wireless without encryption.

What are you talking about?  Both password and public key authentication
methods are secure, regardless of the security of the underlying medium.
All the client has to do is encrypt the password with the server's
public key.  Now, if you don't have the server's public key cached, that
could be the source of a MITM attack.

> >    If you folks who deal with security have suggestions on how to debug this
> > situation, please let me know.
> 
> The logs have already been suggested. `ssh -v` is a tremendous source of
> information, in the worst case stopping `sshd` and then running `sshd -d`
> is another source of information (remember to fully restart sshd when
> done!).

Actually, the best way is to add the '-p' and set up the debugging
server on a separate port.  If you've got to go through a firewall it's
a little more fiddling, but having a working SSH server seems a
requisite for a happy life.

Wil
-- 
Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc>
http://nakedape.cc/wiki/

enabled, that is a source of unsecurity.

From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Wed Dec  6 09:19:57 2006
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Wed, 06 Dec 2006 09:19:57 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] SSH Experts Here?
In-Reply-To: <200612052250.40846.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
References: <200612060317.kB63Hqgk078138@m5p.com>
	<1165385924.31961.5.camel@spartacus.nakedape.priv>
	<200612052250.40846.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
Message-ID: <1165425597.27290.13.camel@willow.odshp.com>

On Tue, 2006-12-05 at 22:50 -0800, Eric Wilhelm wrote:
> # from Wil Cooley
> # on Tuesday 05 December 2006 10:18 pm:
> 
> >Both password and public key authentication
> >methods are secure, regardless of the security of the underlying
> > medium.
> 
> Correct, except that the password can be guessed or gathered.  A 
> password-protected file (your private key) on a hard drive is a bit 
> harder to socially engineer.

I guess that's true; if you're on your own laptop, someone could collect
your password by shoulder-surfing.  If you're trying to use a public
terminal, after you've somehow gotten your key where it can be read by
your SSH client, someone could install a key-logger.  Of course, they
could also install a key-logger that also captured your private key (a
patched Putty, for example).  Which would you rather change, your
password or your private key?

> This thread seems to be about client configuration.  Puzzling.  If the 
> server is listening on a public port with password authentication 
> enabled, that is a source of unsecurity.

It sounds like Rich's issue is enforcing his own practices, not
necessarily wholly blocking external access via password.  If it is,
then you're right.  He'd probably need to run an sshd on a separate port
with a separate config file or disable password access altogether.

Wil
-- 
Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc>
http://nakedape.cc

From alan at clueserver.org  Wed Dec  6 15:29:29 2006
From: alan at clueserver.org (alan)
Date: Wed, 6 Dec 2006 15:29:29 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Anti Ubuntu review...
In-Reply-To: <1165010772.6670.33.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
References: <1165010772.6670.33.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0612061524480.3939@blackbox.fnordora.org>

On Fri, 1 Dec 2006, someone wrote:

> http://blogs.zdnet.com/Bott/?p=105
>
> This person is suggesting that XP Home is better for an old 533 mhz
> laptop than Ubuntu is.  I have a 500 MHz AMD running XP Home on a
> HP Asus P5S-VM mobo configured to sell in Mexico.  I have 512 megs
> of PC100 ram on this machine.
>
> XP doesn't perform at all, it's just barely stable in fact.  Can
> an additional 33 Mhz really make that much difference?  Is ZDnet
> usually anti Linux, or is this review unique?  Couldn't the
> offending network card be replaced by an orinoco b/g gold card?
> Yes, you may have to replace a few components, but getting out of EULA
> hell and avoiding a company that drops security updates in under
> 6 years, Windows ME, seems like a fairly good idea to me.

Not quite.  He is using a cardbus card on a laptop.  I am willing to bet 
that he is running into the secondary pci bus problem.  If he used a 
pcmcia card instead of cardbus it would have worked fine.  (Adding 
"pci=assign-busses" to the kernel line will make the system see the 
cardbus card.)

It is a case of an older machine with known problems that Ubintu does not 
correctly address and no one in the user community who knows where it 
breaks.  (Not an uncommon problem, BTW.)

It is obvious he is just following directions, not trying to understand 
what they mean.

From carla at bratgrrl.com  Wed Dec  6 17:18:21 2006
From: carla at bratgrrl.com (Carla Schroder)
Date: Wed, 6 Dec 2006 17:18:21 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] listing directories only
Message-ID: <200612061718.22047.carla@bratgrrl.com>

Here's an odd one for the wise old geekbeards. I can't seem to find an easy 
way to list directories only. man ls says:

-d, --directory
 list directory entries instead of contents, and do not dereference symbolic 
links

But ls -d does not work:

$ ls -d
.

$ ls -d /home/carla
/home/carla

Good ole find does the job:

$ find ~ -maxdepth 1 -type d  
/home/carla
/home/carla/.kde
/home/carla/.qt
/home/carla/Desktop
/home/carla/.local
[...]

ls will list subdirectories and their contents:

$  ls -R
.:
./downloads:
asterisk-1.4.0-beta3  asterisk-1.4.0-beta3.tar.gz  AstLinux-0.4.4.iso  
debian-testing-i386-netinst.iso  loop

./downloads/asterisk-1.4.0-beta3/agi:
agi-test.agi  DialAnMp3.agi  eagi-sphinx-test.c  eagi-test.c  fastagi-test  
jukebox.agi  Makefile  numeralize

[...]

The tree command is easy and fun:

$ tree -d -L 1
.
|-- 1writing
|-- Desktop
|-- Examples -> /usr/share/example-content
|-- booksnmusic
|-- correspondence
|-- downloads
|-- finances
|-- howto
|-- pictures
|-- sda
`-- webs

So.... does anyone know why ls cannot do this?

--

Linux geek and random computer tamer
check out my Linux Cookbook! 
http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/linuxckbk/
best book for sysadmins and power users

From carla at bratgrrl.com  Wed Dec  6 17:30:44 2006

Goodness, all it takes to do this with ls is the sekeeritt incancation:

[root at asterisk1 sounds]# ls -d */
dictate/  digits/  es/  followme/  fr/  ha/  letters/  local/  phonetic/  
silence/  wx/

From pmvw at outwardfocus.net  Wed Dec  6 17:56:23 2006
From: pmvw at outwardfocus.net (Piet van Weel)
Date: Wed, 6 Dec 2006 16:56:23 -0900
Subject: [PLUG] listing directories only with ls- solved
In-Reply-To: <200612061730.44904.carla@bratgrrl.com>
Message-ID: <000801c719a2$e6fa2ce0$9607a8c0@alpha>

So except for the prettiness what is the difference of doing:

ls -d */

And 

ls * | grep /

Besides one does some prettying up of the output, not much; however, it is
important to note that 

ls --indicator-style=none -d */

Causes ls to append a / to the end of each directory in order to get the
correct output. Despite the not wanting of an indicator style.

Piet

From david.fleck at mchsi.com  Wed Dec  6 18:51:17 2006
From: david.fleck at mchsi.com (David Fleck)
Date: Wed, 6 Dec 2006 20:51:17 -0600 (CST)
Subject: [PLUG] listing directories only with ls- solved

Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0612062046340.5531@grond.sourballs.org>

On Wed, 6 Dec 2006, Piet van Weel wrote:

> So except for the prettiness what is the difference of doing:
> 
> ls -d */
> 
> And 
> 
> ls * | grep /


The only use I've *ever* found for the -d option is as a modifier to 'ls 
-l', when I want to see the permissions/ownership of a directory, but not 
the full listing of everything in the directory.  (Which you could also do 
by 'ls -l' on the parent directory and piping to grep, but it saves a few 
keystrokes.)

E.g., 
 dcf>$ ls -dl sourweb/
 drwxr-xr-x  3 dcf users 4096 Dec 31  2005 sourweb/

 dcf>$ ls -l sourweb/
 total 656K
 -rw-r--r--   1 dcf users   4027 May  7  2000 3.27.txt
 -rw-r--r--   1 dcf users   4602 May  7  2000 5.8.txt
 ...
 ...
 .etc
 ...

--
David Fleck
david.fleck at mchsi.com

From griffint at pobox.com  Wed Dec  6 18:56:13 2006
From: griffint at pobox.com (Terry Griffin)
Date: Wed, 6 Dec 2006 18:56:13 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] listing directories only
In-Reply-To: <200612061718.22047.carla@bratgrrl.com>
References: <200612061718.22047.carla@bratgrrl.com>
Message-ID: <200612061856.13703.griffint@pobox.com>

On Wednesday 06 December 2006 5:18 pm, Carla Schroder wrote:
> Here's an odd one for the wise old geekbeards. I can't seem to find an easy
> way to list directories only. man ls says:
>
> -d, --directory
>  list directory entries instead of contents, and do not dereference
> symbolic links
>
> But ls -d does not work:
>
> $ ls -d
> .
>
> $ ls -d /home/carla
> /home/carla
>
> [snip]
>
> So.... does anyone know why ls cannot do this?

The -d means to list directories as directories instead of recursing
in to them and listing their contents. So 'ls /home/carla' will give you
the contents of /home/carla while 'ls -d /home/carla' will just list
/home/carla. This becomes more evident if you combine -d with some other
option like -l.

  $ ls -l /home/carla

vs:

  $ ls -ld /home/carla

I suppose it's a philosophical thing (keeping ls "small"), but other than
hidden files ls doesn't have any filtering options. It would certainly be
easy to add such options from a programming standpoint, but this has been
left to other utilities like 'find'.

Terry

                             MEETING ANNOUNCEMENT

                        The Portland Linux/Unix Group
                                  will meet
                          7 PM Thursday Dec 7, 2006
                                      at
                          Portland State University
                                      in
                            Smith Memorial Center
                                   Room 294
                           On the block bounded by
       SW Montgomery, SW Broadway (7th), SW Harrison, and SW Park (9th)

      *******************************************************************

                                 PRESENTATION

                            Ingres is Open Source

                                      by

                                 John Murphy
                           <john.murphy at ingres.com>
                                     and
                                    others

      Ingres is a well known Relational Data Base Management
      System similar to Oracle, DB2, and many others.
      Like Oracle, Ingres has been around for a long time,
      has a significant user base and I (David Mandel) believe
      it has more built-in and/or add-on tools than postgres or
      mySQL.

      Ingres has been available on Linux for quite some time,
      but Ingres is now Open Source and Ingres Corporation
      is developing an Open Source straegy for Ingres.

      John Murphy will be in town and will tell us more about Ingres.
      He is also hoping to bring an associate to discuss a new
      generation of Ingres where they bundle a Linux distribution
      from rPath as part of Ingres.

      *******************************************************************

          Agenda:

              7:00 - 7:30  Business
                   We will discuss the status of our ongoing projects
                   including PLUG's monthly Advanced Topics meetings,

John Murphy called me to let us know that Bill Maimone who is Senior
Vice President of Worldwide Engineering at Ingres and a major force
behind open sourcing ingres will be coming to tonight's meeting.  This
is exciting.  I have attached Bill's bio below.

David Mandel
=================================

Bill Maimone

Senior Vice President of Worldwide Engineering

Mr. Maimone leads Ingres's worldwide engineering team in architecting
and delivering the next generation of open source database solutions.
Mr. Maimone brings 20 years of Oracle database architecture management
to Ingres. Most recently, Mr. Maimone served as a vice president in
Oracle's Server Technologies division with responsibility for its
collaboration software, including content services and records
management.

For more than 10 years, he led Oracle's innovation in data security,
including pioneering technology developments and advances within the
U.S. and International Organization for Standardization (ISO) security
bodies. Furthermore, Mr. Maimone's scalability work on both releases of
Oracle7 and Oracle8 databases included real-world record database
scalability achievements.

While at Oracle, Mr. Maimone also managed worldwide, cross-functional
groups of developers working on identity management, SQL language and
runtime services, directory services, globalization, query optimization,
and data dictionary.

Mr. Maimone started his career at Oracle in 1986. He holds both master's
and bachelor's degrees in Computer Science and a bachelor's degree in
Science Journalism from the Massachusetts Institute of Technology.

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Wed Dec  6 16:14:09 2006
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (someone)
Date: Wed, 06 Dec 2006 16:14:09 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Success story...
Message-ID: <1165450449.10851.43.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>

I couldn't get an Iomega PC90 scsi card, appears to have an NCR53c400
chip on it, working under Linux.  So, I picked up an AVA-1502E card,
a.k.a. Iomega zip zoom 16 bit ISA accelerator.  This card, other than
having a Bernoulli come up on it as a Beta 150 under Linux, seems to 
be working perfectly.  I'm guessing the Iomega PC90 card is an
incomplete scsi implementation or the generic NCR driver was incomplete
back when the 2.2.14 Linux kernel came out.  Maybe I can put this on my
father's stand alone Linux fileserver for him now.  

Windows 9x/NT/2000/XP drivers were never written for the Iomega PC90
scsi card, so I won't be surprised if a lot of people have them along
with a Bernoulli drive that they can't use anymore.
My advice, get a scsi card with a compatible external port that 
Linux supports to replace them.  These drives are nicer than zip 100
drives because the disks are 1 1/2 times the capacity of a 100 megabyte
zip disk and there's no external power block.  I did have to change the
slot bracket so I could supply power, I pulled the bracket and power
cable from my Iomega PC90 scsi card.  Iomega built up customer trust 
in Bernoulli disk drives, then Bernoulli got dropped and replaced by 
the incompatible zip drive.  Was this intentional?  Why did zip come
out as a 100 megabyte drive at first if Bernoulli was already up to
150 megabytes?  Perhaps at the time of the zip 100 parallel port drive
craze followed by the push to replace the 1.44 meg floppy drive, the
floppy controller on most PC's should have been replaced with a SCSI-2
controller.

It used to be $10 for a box of 25 floppy disks.  Nowadays, people buy
USB flash keys and they are $30 or more a piece.  Sure the capacity
is awesome, but the cost is very high.

     Michael Robinson



From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Wed Dec  6 17:34:11 2006
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (someone)
Date: Wed, 06 Dec 2006 17:34:11 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Generic Linux
Message-ID: <1165455252.10851.109.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>

Who wants to write to 100 or more different versions of the same
platform?  Is it possible to write an application for Linux that
will work on: Fedora, Ubuntu, Debian, Slackware, etc. equally?

Ideally, a programmer writes to a generic Linux system and 
does not have to concern him/herself about the specifics of 
individual Linux distributions.  This can translate to 
smaller downloads and hopefully fewer bugs.

Why am I bringing this up as a topic?  Well, people go back and forth
about Linux distributions on here a lot.  There seem to be many Linux
distributions and a growing issue with products coming out for one
distribution and not for another.  This should be a problem for 
Windows, but it is less of one because there are far fewer popular
versions of Microsoft's OS around.  

Some people are saying, this week I'm an Ubuntu person and next 
week I'll go back to Fedora or Debian.  Wait a minute, maybe what 
is needed is more standardization across Linux distributions to 
ease migration.

The differences between CentOS and Redhat Enterprise Linux are 
interesting as well.  They supposedly come from the same source
base, but are they really the same?  Is this something someone 
who wants to work with and use Linux should really have to 
worry about?  

Subtopics under the topic, "What is generic Linux?"

1) For distributions that break out because of some proprietary driver,
   has the time come to say get these out of kernel space or don't
   call your offering a Linux system anymore?

2) If a company writes a closed driver, like the commercial nv
   drivers, how does it address Linux distribution diversity 
   today?

3) What can be done about companies that put out proprietary drivers
   for say Fedora Core 6 today when the next two to three versions of 
   Fedora come out in the near future and the libraries those drivers
   depend on change?  Fedora isn't the only example, replace with:
   Ubuntu, Debian, Slackware, etc.  Heck, puppylinux, whatever it is 
   based on, is a popular distribution too.

4) How many Linux distributions should there be?

5) Do the many different Linux distributions mature evenly?

6) Should the total number of Linux distributions be reduced 
   and the communities behind them combined?

There seem to be a growing number of Linux distributions.  Is this
hurting or helping Linux grow?  What can and should be done about
distribution divergence?

     Michael C. Robinson

From znmeb at cesmail.net  Thu Dec  7 19:58:48 2006
From: znmeb at cesmail.net (M. Edward (Ed) Borasky)
Date: Thu, 07 Dec 2006 19:58:48 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Generic Linux
In-Reply-To: <1165455252.10851.109.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
References: <1165455252.10851.109.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <4578E2F8.202@cesmail.net>

someone wrote:
> Who wants to write to 100 or more different versions of the same
> platform?  Is it possible to write an application for Linux that
> will work on: Fedora, Ubuntu, Debian, Slackware, etc. equally?
>   
All of the "major" Linux distributions, assuming the full GNU toolchain, 
compile and execute nearly all stable open-source applications. Where 
the major differences between the distros lie are in how one does system 
administration and how they deal with "non-free" components.
> Some people are saying, this week I'm an Ubuntu person and next 
> week I'll go back to Fedora or Debian.  Wait a minute, maybe what 
> is needed is more standardization across Linux distributions to 
> ease migration.
>   
This week I'm a Gentoo person. Last week I was a Gentoo person. And next 
week I will still be a Gentoo person. The only other distro that I'm 
even considering at this point is rPath.

> The differences between CentOS and Redhat Enterprise Linux are 
> interesting as well.  They supposedly come from the same source
> base, but are they really the same?  Is this something someone 
> who wants to work with and use Linux should really have to 
> worry about?
>   
They are supposed to be "binary compatible" yet distinctly branded. That 
is, Red Hat, because of the GPL, supplies instructions for how one can 
build "Red Hat Trademark-Free" binary packages.

-- 
M. Edward (Ed) Borasky, FBG, AB, PTA, PGS, MS, MNLP, NST, ACMC(P)
http://borasky-research.blogspot.com/

If God had meant for carrots to be eaten cooked, He would have given rabbits fire.

>From: someone <plug_0 at robinson-west.com>
> the incompatible zip drive.  Was this intentional?  Why did zip come
> out as a 100 megabyte drive at first if Bernoulli was already up to
> 150 megabytes?  Perhaps at the time of the zip 100 parallel port drive

Part of this was the Bernoulli media was 5.25" and Zip media was 3.5".

> craze followed by the push to replace the 1.44 meg floppy drive, the
> floppy controller on most PC's should have been replaced with a SCSI-2
> controller.

Indeed, but the time has past and unless you've got a home-built time
machine handy I doubt you'll change that. Seems SCSI's greatest legacy
will be all the copies of it, rather than pure SCSI itself. Both
USB-storage and FireWire are little more than SCSI plus a little bit of
glue. Even SATA looks surprisingly similar to SCSI nowadays...

> It used to be $10 for a box of 25 floppy disks.  Nowadays, people buy
> USB flash keys and they are $30 or more a piece.  Sure the capacity
> is awesome, but the cost is very high.

Then think a tinsy tiny bit more. That box of 25 floppies is roughly 35MB
total. Plus the modern floppy QC is tweaked really low so they can supply
product cheaply enough, leading to horrific reliability.

Now, that USB keyfob might be $30, but it has a capacity of 4GB or more.
Notice how many orders of magnitude larger it is. Plus the keyfob is much
more reliable and faster. By the time you start burning out sectors on
the keyfob you would of gone through many *boxes* of floppies to transfer
the same amount of data due to reliability. Per byte of storage the
keyfob is a *massively* better value.


--

SATA device drivers pretty much build on top of SCSI -- similar to USB
/ SD / SCSI layers.  Probably one of the reasons SCSI is used so much
is, by design, it helps keep the underlying hardware pretty
transparent.  (Linux-wise, at least.)

A few months ago, I was grousing about the lack of waterproof USB
drives.  I found one:

http://www.kingmaxdigi.com/news/news_product/news_p20051114.htm

The Kingmax Superstick drive is tiny - a little blade as big as
the middle of a USB connector, and about 3 centimeters long, and
claims to be waterproof.  I met a friend the other day with a
few of these on a necklace, and I can imagine some artistic type
painting them with interesting patterns and making "art".  I
ordered a couple from overstock.com.  They also claim to have
them at the local P.C. Club stores.  

One will get a washing machine test, and then I will keep both in
my wallet to see how long they last.

From johnxj at comcast.net  Sat Dec  9 19:47:18 2006
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Sat, 9 Dec 2006 19:47:18 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] LPR options
Message-ID: <20061209194718.231ad917.johnxj@comcast.net>

I googled all over, but could not find the answer to the following problem.

I wish to print to Laserjet 8000 from Adobe Reader 7.08 Linux. On linux, Reader displays few options. In fact, doesn't even see installed printers. You have to specify everything at the command line. You can enter the command line options in the print dialog box, and Reader remembers the settings. After considerable futzing around, I found that the following line works:

lpr -P Laserjet-8000-Series-Postscript-(recommended) -# 20

That prints 20 copies, collated, to my Laserjet 8000. The printer name is as installed in CUPS. 

There are a couple problems with the above command line. First, the printer's output stacker will offset each copy. This works by default from Windows, but the above command does not print each copy offset. I can't find any reference on command line options to tell the printer to output the copies offset.

Second, when I print from Windows the printer's control panel tells me what copy is being printed (e.g., "printing copy 17 from tray 4"). When I print from Linux I see no information at all. I know how long each copy takes, so I can use my watch to figure out which copy I am on, but this is  unacceptable. Sometimes I need to go away for a while. It would be nice to know whether I need to pause the print job and add paper before leaving.

Any gurus on LPR commands?


From ehem at m5p.com  Sun Dec 10 12:17:35 2006
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Sun, 10 Dec 2006 12:17:35 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] LPR options
In-Reply-To: <20061210200005.318E641F4@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200612102017.kBAKHZ1W032366@m5p.com>

>From: John Jason Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net>
> lpr -P Laserjet-8000-Series-Postscript-(recommended) -# 20
> 
> That prints 20 copies, collated, to my Laserjet 8000. The printer name is as installed in CUPS. 
> 
> There are a couple problems with the above command line. First, the printer's output stacker will offset each copy. This works by default from Windows, but the above command does not print each copy offset. I can't find any reference on command line options to tell the printer to output the copies offset.
> 
> Second, when I print from Windows the printer's control panel tells me what copy is being printed (e.g., "printing copy 17 from tray 4"). When I print from Linux I see no information at all. I know how long each copy takes, so I can use my watch to figure out which copy I am on, but this is  unacceptable. Sometimes I need to go away for a while. It would be nice to know whether I need to pause the print job and add paper before leaving.
> 
> Any gurus on LPR commands?

I imagine CUPS has other ways of accessing the information, and can give
you more. There are two commands of interested with the old lp interface.
The first is `lpq` (use the same -P option as for lpr), this will give
you the status of the print queue. Modern printers buffer data, so lpq
won't give you a perfect picture, merely some, it will tell you about
running out of paper though. The second is `lpc` (use the -P option as
above), this is for control. lpc will allow you to stop the printer to
add paper, though I've found using the buttons on the front of the
printer tends to work pretty well too.  :-)

From jonscully at gmail.com  Sun Dec 10 15:31:03 2006
From: jonscully at gmail.com (Jon Scully)
Date: Sun, 10 Dec 2006 15:31:03 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Cisco 678
In-Reply-To: <727fc2ee0612101501r64e87522hee0786fd775154d0@mail.gmail.com>
References: <727fc2ee0612101501r64e87522hee0786fd775154d0@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <53107f6e0612101531k130a13ech3b9b6d7bb0bfe300@mail.gmail.com>

If you need port 22 (ssh) to be directed to an internal box, say,
192.168.0.15, at the enable prompt of your 678, enter:

    set nat entry add 192.168.0.15 22 tcp

HTH

On 12/10/06, User Iam <vramnum10 at gmail.com> wrote:
> Well, I did that....
>
> And then sshed to another site and tried to ssh back...
>
> Can't

- Your ssh server could be broken:
  - maybe it's not running
  - maybe you typed your password wrong
  - ....
- you could have the ip address(es) wrong..
....
- the other site you ssh'd to could be using a broken ssh client, or
something... maybe *that* site is blocking _outbound_ ssh.
- The FSM could have decided it's just your bad day, and intervened
with it's noodly appendage to interrupt your computer activities.

"Can't" doesn't help us help you at all.  *PLEASE* pass along the
error mesages, or at least an explanation of what you tried and what
happened.

--Rogan

From johnxj at comcast.net  Sun Dec 10 19:05:07 2006
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Sun, 10 Dec 2006 19:05:07 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] LPR options
In-Reply-To: <200612102017.kBAKHZ1W032366@m5p.com>
References: <20061210200005.318E641F4@ulke.nakedape.cc>
	<200612102017.kBAKHZ1W032366@m5p.com>
Message-ID: <20061210190507.5af13bb1.johnxj@comcast.net>

On Sun, 10 Dec 2006 12:17:35 -0800 (PST)
Elliott Mitchell <ehem at m5p.com> dijo:

> >From: John Jason Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net>
> > lpr -P Laserjet-8000-Series-Postscript-(recommended) -# 20
> > 
> > That prints 20 copies, collated, to my Laserjet 8000. The printer name is as installed in CUPS. 
> > 
> > There are a couple problems with the above command line. First, the printer's output stacker will offset each copy. This works by default from Windows, but the above command does not print each copy offset. I can't find any reference on command line options to tell the printer to output the copies offset.
> > Second, when I print from Windows the printer's control panel tells me what copy is being printed (e.g., "printing copy 17 from tray 4"). When I print from Linux I see no information at all. I know how long each copy takes, so I can use my watch to figure out which copy I am on, but this is  unacceptable. Sometimes I need to go away for a while. It would be nice to know whether I need to pause the print job and add paper before leaving.

> I imagine CUPS has other ways of accessing the information, and can give
> you more. There are two commands of interested with the old lp interface.
> The first is `lpq` (use the same -P option as for lpr), this will give
> you the status of the print queue. Modern printers buffer data, so lpq
> won't give you a perfect picture, merely some, it will tell you about
> running out of paper though. The second is `lpc` (use the -P option as
> above), this is for control. lpc will allow you to stop the printer to
> add paper, though I've found using the buttons on the front of the
> printer tends to work pretty well too.  :-)

Thanks for the reply. After writing the above I fiddled with things
some more and finally gave up. I can print with job offset from most
other Linux applications, just not Adobe Reader 7.08. The display also
tells me which copy is being printed when I print from other
applications too.

The problem is the lame print dialog box in Adobe Reader 7.08. It is
not aware of CUPS. All it give me is a command line to enter an lp or
lpr command. I finally figured out enough to get basic output, but I'm
afraid I'd have to study lp and lpr a lot to figure out how to make it
do job offset. It may not even be possible. Job offset is pretty much
available only for large printers that have an output stacker. It may
be that no one ever created a command for this in lpr. And if there is
such a command, finding it in the documentation may be a challenge.
Very few people would ever want or need such a command.

Kpdf prints with job offset just fine. It is also CUPS-aware and
therefore has a decent print dialog box. The only reason I want to
print from Adobe Reader is because Kpdf prints the first copy
agonizingly slow. After all the effort I put into figuring out how to
get job offset from Adobe Reader I've decided it isn't worth any more
effort. Someday Adobe may fix the print dialog box. Meanwhile, I'm
going to put up with Kpdf.

As for pausing the printer, etc., I agree. The only reason I'd want to
pause the printer is to add paper, change a toner cart, or something
requiring me to be at the printer. As long as I'm there, hitting the
Online/Offline button is faster than trying to figure out a software
command.

Subject: [PLUG] Ready for website creation tutorials
Message-ID: <20061210194444.2f507245.johnxj@comcast.net>

Some time back I expressed interest in creating a website for students
to practice Phonetics. Here is a site with various exercises
illustrating the kind of thing I want to do:

http://www.umanitoba.ca/faculties/arts/linguistics/russell/138/course.htm

(You have to try out some of the exercises to see what I mean.)

I also want to create a website that mimics the sample editable PDF
file that I created and posted here:

http://www.web.pdx.edu/~johj

The editable PDF file there did not work because I was unable to embed
a font for the controls. I used OOo, which sets the font for all
controls to Helvetica on PDF export, ignoring the font the user set for
the control. There are no plans to change this behavior. I also tried
Scribus, but it allows only five fonts for controls, none of which have
all the IPA characters. Scribus may correct this soon, but in the
meantime I am thinking that a web page may be a better tool. I like the
idea of the student being able to click on a "Correct my work" button
and having the web page give immediate feedback.

So now exams are over and I have some time to learn a bit about web
programming so that (maybe) I can figure out how to create a site to
provide students practice in Phonetics. Initially, I just want to
create two pages: One which will list the features of a sound with a
drop-down or other means for the student to enter the answer; and a
second one which will be the same except that instead of a description
of the sound it will have a link to a sound file. Later I may try other
types of quizzes and exercises. To accomplish this --

1) I need to figure out how to make sure the students' browsers can see
the IPA characters. My browsers on my Ubuntu computer (Firefox and
Opera) have no problem. But I know that many students have reported to
me that they do not see the characters I post in PSU WebCT. I think the
problem is related to ISO-8859 v. UTF-8, but I am unclear how to fix
it. I need to create a Help button on the web page that will pop up
instructions for students on how to fix their browser so that it
displays UTF-8. And if installing a font is required, I need to figure
that out as well. I suppose I will have to give instructions for IE,
Firefox and Opera, on the three main platforms, Windows, Linux and Mac.
If anyone knows of a site or resources to help me get this together, it
would be appreciated.

2) I need to learn web programming sufficient to do this project. While
at the mall today I stopped at Barnes and Noble and browsed through a
couple books on website creation. They were "for dummies" books. I was
able to understand them, but they were aimed toward creating a
commercial site with a shopping cart and stuff. Can anyone give me a
hint where to start for the kind of site I want to create? Books to
start with? Websites with tutorials for beginners? And on this topic,
what kinds of programs do I need to use (Linux, of course)?

Subject: [PLUG] soundfile formats hurting head
Message-ID: <200612102013.54893.carla@bratgrrl.com>

hey all,

My google-fu has failed me- Asterisk gives three choices for the format for 
recording voicemail files: wav49, gsm, and wav. wav49 is GSM-compressed wave- 
both it and gsm are highly compressed, about one-tenth the size of wav files, 
and are good-enough for voice recordings. wav is the best sound quality, but 
it's way too fat. I'd like to use just wav49.

What I'm having trouble finding out is what client systems support them? wav 
is everywhere, but is wav49 well-supported on Linux and Mac OSX? Can OSX and 
windoze play gsm files without major tweakage?

thanks!

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Mon Dec 11 01:27:24 2006
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Mon, 11 Dec 2006 01:27:24 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Re: Damned small and waterproof USB drives
In-Reply-To: <20061209024654.GA32394@gate.kl-ic.com>
References: <20061209024654.GA32394@gate.kl-ic.com>
Message-ID: <20061211092724.GA5483@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Fri, Dec 08, 2006 at 06:46:54PM -0800, Keith Lofstrom wrote:
> 
> A few months ago, I was grousing about the lack of waterproof USB
> drives.  I found one:
> 
> http://www.kingmaxdigi.com/news/news_product/news_p20051114.htm
> 
> The Kingmax Superstick drive is tiny - a little blade as big as
> the middle of a USB connector, and about 3 centimeters long, and
> claims to be waterproof.  I met a friend the other day with a
> few of these on a necklace, and I can imagine some artistic type
> painting them with interesting patterns and making "art".  I
> ordered a couple from overstock.com.  They also claim to have
> them at the local P.C. Club stores.  
> 
> One will get a washing machine test, and then I will keep both in
> my wallet to see how long they last.

Okay, I got one.  Important learnings:

1)  It does just fine in a washing machine - double washed.  However,
I made the mistake of anchoring it inside my blue jeans with a safety
pin, and the stress of that broke off the little end bit with the hole
to hold the anchor loop.  So it is now 1.2 inches long instead of 1.34
inches long!

The break revealed the potting compound - the device is well embedded
in solid plastic, appearing to be the same sort used for integrated
circuit packages.  The seal was not compromised, so the data storage
is not affected.  I will keep using it, but ...

2) There are two kinds of Superstick - the regular (white) and the
Superstick Pro (blue).  I have the regular.  It reads very quickly,
tens of megabytes per second, but it writes ever so slowly - maybe
eight megabytes per minute.  So a 2GB regular Superstick takes hours
to write!  The Superstick Pro claims to write at 13MB/second, almost
100x faster, and one review I've seen supports this.

3) There do not appear to be any domestic sources of the Superstick
Pro;  they are only available in Taiwan and Hong Kong.  That may
change over time, but if anyone wants the fast ones now, they will
have to find a way to import them.  Pat Heiden of PCH Cables imports
a lot of stuff from China, perhaps we can talk him into getting some.

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

John-
You could do this in any number of languages. My personal preference is PHP
because its easy and quick to learn. Most of the books @ Barnes and Noble
are a good fit (O'Reilly especially) .. I'm beginning to learn Ruby which
has built in Ajax support so you could make the site really spiffy if you
wanted to go that route, or python which has a nice web-tutorial so you
don't end up spending any money http://www.diveintopython.org

I'm not sure about the character encoding. I'll do some research and see
what I can come up with :-)

Drew-

check what your public IP address is being mapped to by going to
http://www.portforward.com/
It shows up at the bottom of the page.

How are you able to determine the 678's IP address at all?  (e.g.
'show int wan0-0' shows the Destination IP -- not the router's IP)

One way to determine your IP address is, from inside your LAN, go to
one of the many free (free-as-in-advertising) "What's My IP Address"
sites, such as: http://www.ipaddressworld.com/

HTH
