On Mon, 18 Dec 2006 07:46:40 -0800
Carla Schroder <carla at bratgrrl.com> dijo:

> Actually, John should probably run the restart command on his computer.

:) :) :)

Actually, it wasn't necessary because I had to shut down to go to the
Clinic yesterday anyway. But I have saved the restart command as I will
no doubt be using it again.

When I shut down the computer I also discovered something else about
this problem. I was printing 100 copies of a fairly large book from
Adobe Reader. There are two ways to do this. If you use the GUI, Reader
will concatenate individual print jobs into one massive spool file. And
then each job will be printed de novo, that is, I lose the speed
benefit of "RIP once, print many." Now, Adobe Reader does print pretty
quickly, but I prefer to get as much speed as possible out of the
printer. So instead of that option I used the command line "lp -P
<printername> -# 100." This was working fine and I thought that it was
doing it by "RIP once, print many" because when I executed the command
it spooled just one copy and was done. Well, when I shut down the
computer to go to the Clinic, the printer suddenly stopped.

Yep, the lp command was sending the job over and over quietly in the
background. I suppose that's better than making a massive spool file,
but it doesn't give me the speed increase benefit from "RIP once, print
many," plus I have to leave the computer connected.

Because I was in a hurry to get to the Clinic I didn't have time to
futz with it further and I needed the printer running while I was off
playing Linux geek. I had to go to the Windows computer and resend the
print job from Adobe Reader there. 

So now I have no solution for printing these jobs properly from Linux.
Kpdf works great, just like Adobe Reader from Windows, except its first
copy out is glacially slow. I mean sometimes two or three minutes per
page. Canon WX engine printers like mine tend to spill toner when the
printer doesn't run at a reasonable clip, so the first copy out is
messed up, plus this tends to cause jams. But at least it does "RIP
once, print many," uses job offset, shows in the printer control panel
which copy is being printed and which tray it is being printed from.
Adobe Reader can't do any of those things, but prints extremely fast.
There is a button on the print dialog box that says "Optimize for
speed." It is checked by default. I don't know how Adobe does this or
why Kpdf apparently does not.

Evince and all the other PDF viewers have lame print dialog boxes that
don't offer the options that I need.

My next attack on this issue will be to wander over to the KDE forums
and wikis and stuff and see if I can find out why Kpdf is so slow.

I should also mention that the printer has genuine imitation Adobe
PostScript level 2. I know little of the relationship between PDF and
PostScript, except that PDF is somehow a streamlined and viewable
version of PostScript. So maybe there is a way to bypass the viewers
and just send the file to the printer. I know I can create a PostScript
print file and then send that to the printer. But the PostScript print
file would need the features set properly to give me "RIP once, print
many," job offset, and all the other goodies this printer is capable of
doing.

Meantime, any further suggestions are welcome.

From tkubaska at charter.net  Mon Dec 18 09:21:02 2006

From alan.olsen at gmail.com  Mon Dec 18 09:29:11 2006
From: alan.olsen at gmail.com (Alan Olsen)
Date: Mon, 18 Dec 2006 09:29:11 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] missing characters in PDF
In-Reply-To: <20061218172104.PHMW1448.aa01.charter.net@urchin>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0612180745200.5531@grond.sourballs.org>
	<20061218172104.PHMW1448.aa01.charter.net@urchin>
Message-ID: <55cf303a0612180929t66797504oc370bc0ae7c2a21b@mail.gmail.com>

On 12/18/06, TedK <tkubaska at charter.net> wrote:
>
> I downloaded a PDF to read and mostly it's fine. But it is missing some
> characters, replacing them with blanks. These characters are probably
> special characters (Greek symbols in equations).
>
> My question is, Is this my problem or the PDF's. Do I not have some fonts
> installed or configured on my system? Or is it a problem with the way the
> PDF was made? Thanks.


Sounds like the fonts were not embedded in the pdf.

Many fonts have the "no embed" flag set to prevent them from being included
in documents.  (It is an "anti-copying scheme" by the font manufacturers.
It is pretty easy to bypass if you know which bits to twiddle.)

If the pdf does not have the font available, it will try and guess.  Not all
fonts have all of the proper characters.  (The usual example of this is the
weird upper ascii character that MS-Word uses for an apostrophe.)

If you can find the original font name, you can try to get a copy of it.
Linux will handle TrueType fonts just fine.

Different formulations, from what I can tell.  (For one thing, you get less
dpi from a xerox machine.  About 72dpi, in my experience.)

Old xeroxes seem to hold up better (and not stick together) than the old
laser prints.

It may just depend on who made the machine and the type of toner used.

On Mon, 2006-12-18 at 06:10 -0800, Rich Shepard wrote:
>    My problem now is that two perl modules (BerkeleyDB and Mail::SPF::Query)
> fail one test each and will not install. I've no idea if the failed tests
> are relavant to my need, nor how to handle this within CPAN. The BerkeleyDB
> module is needed for postgrey (the grey listing addon to postfix), but I've
> found what appears to be a better grey listing implementation so I am no
> longer interested in that one. I do need the other module, however, and will
> probably get the source from the repository and build it myself.

Last I checked, BerkleyDB (as in DB_File) was part of the core perl
distribution.  It Just Works(tm).  You may be missing the actualy
libraries that handle the db interactions (as in, not perl stuff), so
the API fails...

Mail::SPF::Query I've no real experience with, but a good starting point
is what particular error message you're getting.  CPAN sometimes doesn't
handle dependencies well.  If you're getting an error message like
"Can't Locate Foo/Bar.pm in @INC (@INC contains.....)" message, you need
to install the Foo::Bar module.  

-Dave

>
> Even if you do have the tools already installed, it may be worth
> re-installing them.  I've always had the suspicion that they interact
> with the VMWare instance somehow.  It would be interesting to know if
> that does have an effect, in any case.


It depends on the OS.  With Windows as a guest OS, you should just be able
to install the toolkit and resolve the issue.  (I believe it also installs a
video driver for the guest.)  For a Linux guest OS, you need to recompile it
for each time you upgrade the kernel.  (It does not replace the video driver
since that part is included with X. It does effect the clock, however.)

You can create VMWare images with the free VMWare server version.

BTW, I am typing this using VMWare player running an image of Fedora Core 5
under Windows.

Ed,

I've had good luck with APC.  I'd suggest looking at Network UPS Tools
(NUT) and finding out which modules it supports and, if you're so
inclined, give preference to companies supporting the project.

http://www.networkupstools.org/

In addition to manager and agents for shutting systems down, it provides
a web interface and a command-line interface.  At one job, I had an
add-in card from APC that monitored temperature and humidity and it
provided that data also.

At one time, I had Net-SNMP configured to use the CLI to make the data
available through SNMP and graphed it with Cricket.  I still have the
top-level Cricket config file and a sample host file.

Wil

From aaron at bavariati.org  Tue Dec 19 17:29:13 2006
From: aaron at bavariati.org (Aaron Burt)
Date: Tue, 19 Dec 2006 17:29:13 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] WipeInfo Equivalent
In-Reply-To: <45888BAA.7030705@comcast.net>
References: <45888449.4030200@comcast.net> <45888720.5030608@acm.org>
	<45888BAA.7030705@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <20061220012913.GD8953@syrinxpc.com>

On Tue, Dec 19, 2006 at 05:02:34PM -0800, Richard C. Steffens wrote:
> Roderick A. Anderson wrote:
> 
> > Not sure if it is included on Knoppix but all my Redhatian based systems
> > have shred.
> 
> > The man page mentions issue with several file systems but I don't think
> > it would be a problem for you.  That is if you're planning on a complete
> > wipe of the partition(s)?
> 
> No. They still want XP running when I'm done. They just want to erase a
> lot of data.

Five things:
1. That's a strange thing to ask for on a Linux/Unix Group list.
2. That said, you should be able to use a Linux bootcd and shred.
3. If you want a 'Doze-ish solution, google for secure delete windows.
4. That said, there'll be enough of the data lying around from older,
   deleted versions of the files that you'd best just wipe with dd or
   badblocks and reinstall.  Winders needs regular reinstalls anyway.
5. If you can't reinstall Winders because of license issues, might I
   suggest some nice Linux desktops?

You can use it to zap all the unallocated blocks on the disk too.

From tonyr at hevanet.com  Tue Dec 19 18:08:34 2006
From: tonyr at hevanet.com (tonyr)
Date: Tue, 19 Dec 2006 18:08:34 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] WipeInfo Equivalent
In-Reply-To: <20061220012913.GD8953@syrinxpc.com>

	<20061220012913.GD8953@syrinxpc.com>
Message-ID: <45889B22.5010407@hevanet.com>

Note that XP uses NTFS. Linux has been weak in NTFS read/write
support until recently (ntfs-3g). The last thing I read about ntfs-3g
was that is was still in beta. Norton Wipe Info seems to be able
to handle directories (recursive, depth-first wipe?). I don't see the same
facility in 'shred' (single files only?) just looking at the command line
options, but I guess you could use 'find' to duplicate that.

- tony

At this point, I want to pipe in my concerns about greylisting and
SPF.  

SPF doesn't appear to be as widely adopted as it needs to
be to meet it's full potential.  I've run into advice googling 
that NAT makes helo/ehlo filtering inappropriate. I wonder how 
NAT affects SPF?  

If you have four NAT routers and your mail server is behind them, you
could end up trying to connect to a greylisting smtp server from
potentially four different ip addresses.  By then, your email has
probably bounced back to you.

Multiple domains on a single subnet I understand is not uncommon.  In
this kind of an environment, does the provider have to control the SPF
records for every single hosted domain?

Why are reverse dns PTR zones only 1:1, yet forward dns zones are at
least many:1?  Mapping 1:many seems to be logically needed if mapping
many:1 is allowed.

I don't know if common SPF implementations use: reverse DNS, whois,
and/or forward dns.  I'm assuming forward DNS on the sender from
address looking for an SPF record to try and verify that an 
SMTP server authorized for the domain of that address is the 
current client.  Someone please correct me if I'm wrong.
I'm assuming an IP address is gained from the SPF record which is
compared to the client SMTP server's IP address.



...A WHOIS EXAMPLE...
BTW, how does whois on something like foo at 1.2.3.4
actually work?  Let's say 1.2.3.4 is mapped to the bad.example.org
domain so that doing whois foo at bad.example.org and whois foo at 1.2.3.4
return the same name servers and contact information.  Does this work
right if multiple domains:1 subnet is considered?  Say gimmeabreak.org
is also hosted on the same subnet that bad.example.org is on.  Let's
say you have the ip address from gimmeabreak.org, but not the domain
name.  Let's say there's an email from an address of
fred at nat2.gimmeabreak.lan coming from an ip address of 1.2.3.4. 
Authenticating the latter is not an uncommon issue in my experience.
Because of NAT, unlisted addresses can't just be discarded.  The
remote smtp server should have masqueraded fred at nat2.gimmeabreak.lan
to fred at gimmeabreak.org, right?  



...CAN POSTFIX BE MADE NAT AWARE?...
Can Postfix masquerade from and to addresses based on which network
it is trying to reach?  For example, fred at nat2.gimmeabreak.lan is
a valid source on private network A, but the from needs to change 
to fred at gimmeabreak.org for going to an Internet destination or
fread at nat2.outofhismind.wan for going to a private destination on 
say network B.

Postfix often has to be set up in a special way on hosting sites that
use it.  Virtual machine hosting, like one sees at godaddy.com, is
becoming more popular though.


     -- Michael C. Robinson

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Wed Dec 20 10:06:59 2006
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Wed, 20 Dec 2006 10:06:59 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Version Control and Open Office Documents
Message-ID: <20061220180659.GA7562@gate.kl-ic.com>


How about a mashup of Open Office with a version control system, like
GIT or Subversion?

Open Office Writer has a function "Edit - Compare Document", which
does a function similar to the merge operations of GIT or SVN.  Open
Office can be controlled from the command line, using the API, so it
should be possible to do differencing and merging from a hook script
in the version control system.  This would make Open Office documents
as compatable with version control as plain text files, and provide a
powerful incentive for organizations to move from Word to Open Office.

I imagine consultants could make big bucks teaching organizations how
to do this.  Anyone interested in helping me figure this out?

Keith

From russell at personaltelco.net  Wed Dec 20 13:37:46 2006
From: russell at personaltelco.net (Russell Senior)
Date: 20 Dec 2006 13:37:46 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] gah, novice performs vigorous foot-perforation with git!
Message-ID: <868xh2s0yd.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>


So, fresh of the inspiring presentation of git, I am busy trying it
out to help organize a bunch of data we collected along with some
analytic infrastructure.  I put the main infrastructure into the
master branch, in a ${ROOT}/dev directory, and a bunch of
lab/calibration type data into a branch called test-data, in a
${ROOT}/data directory.  I am going to put some field data into
another branch.  Okay, so I want the infrastructure scripts available
to the various branches, so I try to remember what git command I am
supposed to use, and get-rebase sounds right.  I checkout the
test-data branch with:

  git-checkout test-data

Then apply:

  git-rebase master test-data

I resolve a few merge conflicts (or I think I do, remember I am a
novice).  I type:

  $ git-rebase -v --continue
  You must edit all merge conflicts and then
  mark them as resolved using git update-index  

Okay, so I do what the man says, and I:

  $ git update-index .gitignore dev/scripts/generate-report.sh

and then for kicks:

  $ git-rebase -v --continue

  Applying 'added some more local processing tweaks'

  No changes - did you forget update-index?

  When you have resolved this problem run "git rebase --continue".
  If you would prefer to skip this patch, instead run "git rebase --skip".
  To restore the original branch and stop rebasing run "git rebase --abort".

Hmm, update-index?  Didn't I just do that?  Okay, now we are coming to
the stupid part.  I type:

  $ git-status     
  # On branch refs/heads/test-data
  # Untracked files:
  #   (use "git add" to add to commit)
  #
  #       .dotest/
  nothing to commit

I think to myself: "hmm, i'm done with that, aren't I?" and poof:

  $ rm -rf .dotest

then for good measure:

  $ git-rebase -v master test-data

which is what I started out with.  

Oops.  Where is ${ROOT}/data???

Heh.  I assume it is in .git/objects somewhere, but I'm thinking now
that "rm -rf .dotest" wasn't on the Official Souvenir Program.
git-log pretends not to know anything about my data directory.

No real worries, as this was experimental and I have all the original
copies, but is there a way I can turn back the time machine to before
I machine-gunned my foot?

TIA for any helpful clues.


-- 
Russell Senior, Secretary
russell at personaltelco.net

Yesterday I went away for a few hours, but I left my Dapper laptop
running. I usually do leave it running, and I have no automatic
shutdown settings that I know of. For example, I leave it running when
I go to bed, and in the morning it is still running. The only thing
that kicks in is the screensaver.

When I came home it was completely turned off. I immediately assumed
something had gone flakey with the power brick and the battery had run
down as a result. But when I restarted it the battery was fully charged.

I would never leave it without being sure all open files had been
saved, so I didn't lose anything. Nevertheless, it is disconcerting.
This has never happened before. I'd like to know what made it suddenly
decide to shut itself down. Does anyone have any ideas?

From tkubaska at charter.net  Wed Dec 20 16:09:52 2006
From: tkubaska at charter.net (tkubaska at charter.net)
Date: Wed, 20 Dec 2006 16:09:52 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Here is a simple MySQL question
Message-ID: <586285960.1166659792792.JavaMail.root@fepweb11>

Here's a simple MySQL question. I've been working through an example and discovered that the downloaded sql scripts do not work. Should they work as given?

I can modify them to work. I must give the default value for a column as the same type as the column. The book I'm reading is "Multi-Tier Application Programming with PHP" by David Wall. It's fairly new although as you can see below it does use Type instead of Engine (which works OK).

This is what I must do, however...

Here is what is in the download ....
DROP TABLE IF EXISTS ACCT_account;

CREATE TABLE `ACCT_account`
(`id` int NOT NULL AUTO_INCREMENT ,
`name` varchar(100) NOT NULL DEFAULT '' ,
`parent` int DEFAULT '' ,
PRIMARY KEY (`id`))
TYPE=InnoDB;

Here is how I must modify it to work ...
DROP TABLE IF EXISTS ACCT_account;

CREATE TABLE `ACCT_account`
(`id` int NOT NULL AUTO_INCREMENT ,
`name` varchar(100) NOT NULL DEFAULT '' ,
`parent` int DEFAULT 0 ,
PRIMARY KEY (`id`))
TYPE=InnoDB;

I was just fighting something very similar on a Windows based install of
MySQL (shudder). I'm going to make as assumption and say you're using MySQL
5.x correct? If so it has to do with Strict Checking. I haven't installed
5.x on any of my linux boxen yet so I can't tell you how to disable it but
that should be the trick.

This used to work on mysql 5.0.2 without throwing an error - but has 
been 'corrected' by mysql 5.0.24 most likely to be more ANSI compliant.

possibility would be for it to be default NULL).  The change you've made 
will not negatively affect any higher level development.

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Wed Dec 20 16:21:51 2006
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Wed, 20 Dec 2006 16:21:51 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Here is a simple MySQL question

Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0612201616440.16047@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Wed, 20 Dec 2006, tkubaska at charter.net wrote:

> CREATE TABLE `ACCT_account`
> (`id` int NOT NULL AUTO_INCREMENT ,
> `name` varchar(100) NOT NULL DEFAULT '' ,
> `parent` int DEFAULT '' ,
> PRIMARY KEY (`id`))
> TYPE=InnoDB;

   I know nothing about MySqueal, but in PostgreSQL and SQLite3 there is no
quoting around the names of variables. So the above would be written:

   create table ACCT_account (id interger not null auto_increment,
                              name varchar(100) not null default ' ',
 			     parent integer default NULL,
 			     primary key (id));

   Notice that your parent field is an integer but you're assigning an empty
string as the default. You should assign a number or NULL if the poor field
doesn't know who its parent is.

HTH,

Rich

On Wed, 20 Dec 2006, John Jason Jordan wrote:

> Dec 18 14:17:29 localhost kernel: [75351.528067] opera[21469]: segfault

   Perhaps the fat lady has sung.

> Also, how does one run fsck? You can't run it on a mounted partition,
> right? But I have to be booted into Linux to use it. Is the normal
> procedure to use a live CD?

   Or, as root, switch to run level 1 (single user mode) by typing 'telinit
1' (without the quotes). After the system shuts down multiuser mode (run
levels 3 and 5, usually), you'll get a root login prompt. Login and see
what's mounted. You can then unmount a partition and run the appropriate
fsck on it.

   When you're all finished, you can 'telinit 3' (or 'telinit 5') and return
to what you were doing.

Rich

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Wed Dec 20 19:32:51 2006
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Wed, 20 Dec 2006 19:32:51 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Running fsck
In-Reply-To: <20061220162156.518fd232.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20061220134221.d96d7e0a.johnxj@comcast.net>
	<4589BFB5.7050005@comcast.net>
	<20061220162156.518fd232.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <20061221033251.GA12137@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Wed, Dec 20, 2006 at 04:21:56PM -0800, John Jason Jordan wrote:
> 
> Also, how does one run fsck? You can't run it on a mounted partition,
> right? But I have to be booted into Linux to use it. Is the normal
> procedure to use a live CD?

There are two standard ways.  They involve a text window, as root.

1)  Bring up a text window.  change user to root:
  [john]$  su - root
  Password:  YourRootPasswordHere
  [root]#  touch /forcefsck
  [root]#  reboot            (or just do it with the GUI)

2)  Bring up a text window.  change user to root:
  [john]$  su - root
  Password:  YourRootPasswordHere
  [root]#  /sbin/shutdown -F -r

Either of these will cause the system to fsck ALL the partitions
when it restarts (otherwise, the root partition can't be fixed).
Note that this can take a LONG time, proportional to the ratio of
disk size over the machine speed, about 15 minutes on my 80GByte,
1.6GHz laptop.

If you have the time to spare, and nothing critical coming up for
the next day or two, it is a good idea to do this every two or
three months.  While we like to brag about how long Linux can go
without rebooting, most file systems are set up to force an fsck
after 180 days without a reboot, or after 30 reboots.  The times
when you really need to reboot to fix something, you will probably
be in a hurry, and waiting an extra 15 minutes for an fsck at those
times is NOT a happy experience.

However, if you have not fsck'ed for a while, and really need to
reboot quickly, you can do a:
  [root]#  /sbin/shutdown -f -r    (lower case "f")
and it will prevent an fsck for the next reboot.  Don't do that
too often, or inevitable bit rot will give your hard drive gangrene.

Oh, and do backups.  See "www.dirvish.org" for my favorite method ...

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993

System Administration section Date & Time ->
Enter administrator mode with the button on the lower right ->
Select timezone ->
Close

Well, my brother bought Wordperfect 8 for Linux.  Corel ran into trouble
shortly after that though. 

Why did your client upgrade from Windows 98 anyways?  The problem
with XP is that WPA is a royal pain in the butt.  Hardware
fingerprinting is just plain wrong, Microsoft is trying to restrict
legit use more than piracy.  This will be especially true if Vista
refuses to boot after say it's main partition has been accessed by
an NT 4.0 system.  For Microsoft to say, "we'll give people a way
around WPA on XP if we feel like it," that is not very encouraging.
Microsoft obviously concerned that people won't migrate to Vista is
not going to relax the XP licensing scheme.  All I can say if you 
have an activated copy of XP and you forsee needing to use it in 
the future is, learn about MondoBackup and qtparted.  Install Linux 
to the second half of the drive and be aware that you won't be able 
to do any major hardware changes on this system anymore.

XP fingerprints the burned in MAC address on the network card and
probably about four other hardware characteristics as well.  I
don't know if XP Pro fingerprints, but why spend $400 for a legit
copy of it?

When they clean up the language on their site and get going again,
you might want to refer these clients to http://www.reactos.org/.
The project at the moment appears to be failing.  If a grass
roots funding campaign gets started, maybe it will breathe new 
life into it.  I don't see ReactOS as a system anyone should 
standardize on, but if the project can get stable sooner it 
might provide a nice temporary drop in replacement for people 
needing an alternate migration path who don't want to go from 
XP to Vista.  Eventually, everyone in XP now will lose it.

The best way to for sure erase delted files is to mirror your hard drive
shortly after you install XP to another one and destroy the one you
copied when it's time to ensure that the files are gone.  This technique
requires that hard drives be bought in pairs.  Destruction via
sledgehammer works quite well.

Seriously though, Microsoft, if the rumors on 
http://www.linux-ntfs.org/ are true, is moving to give fair use the
biggest black eye in the history of software licensing.  There are a
number of shops with custom NT4.0 software that will probably try to
access a Vista partition from NT4.  Microsoft is apparently trying to
halt the use of NT with Vista.

I've heard credible rumors that Microsoft redid the network stack in
Vista because the Windows 2000 stack was directly lifted from BSD.  
I wonder if Microsoft violated any BSD licensing terms and is trying
to cover that up?

Why did the world get into the whole NTFS mess in the first place?
Why did companies pay for Microsoft to shove the whole world into 
a proprietary file system?  Sure, fat filesystems are terrible.
You'd think anyone with a large budget in need of reliable systems
is smart enough to avoid vendor lock in.  Microsoft is attempting 
to achieve vendor lock in on an enormous scale now.  Where is the
resistance to this?  Is MacOS-X cursed with a WPA scheme now or do
you only have to provide a code?

     --  Michael C. Robinson

With Fedora releasing at such a rapid rate, how can one keep their
own ftp or NFS Fedora archive up to date?  How do you create your own
yum mirror?  So far, I'm only putting the odd numbered Fedora releases
on my ftp site.  It would help as far as updates if Fedora legacy
would periodically rerelease older Fedora releases with the updates
worked into them.  Instead of maintaining updates with releases, one
could host the most recent rerelease.  This would save space.  A patch
against the last iso could used to update to a rerelease reducing the
amount one has to download.  At the pace that Fedora replaces itself,
rerelease patches could save a lot of bandwidth.

From baloo at ursine.ca  Wed Dec 20 11:12:12 2006
From: baloo at ursine.ca (Paul Johnson)
Date: Wed, 20 Dec 2006 11:12:12 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Re: UPS
References: <4587D8C9.6020407@alcpress.com>
Message-ID: <p45r54xk3e.ln2@ursa-major.ursine.ca>

Ed Sawicki wrote:

> I'm trying to choose UPS makes and models that I will
> support in the Linux systems I install at customer sites.
> I know there are many that include the vendor's software
> for Linux. However, I'm interested in those that allow me
> to monitor the UPS programatically, such as /proc entries,
> an API, or something similar. Software that only allows a
> human to interact with a GUI display is useless to me.
> Any personal experiences?

Pretty much all APC battery backup units (I assume that's what you meant,
since uninterruptable power supplies are usually diesel powered and take up
a crapload of space) are Linux-friendly with a variety of OSS software
available to read status and log power information with.  I don't know why
other manufacturers even bother at this point... APC's are generally
reasonably priced, reliable and they're drop in, plug-n-play, the batteries
are of a standard type and readily replaced, and they just work.  I really
can't think of anything anybody could possibly want out of a power supply
that most APC units don't offer by default.

From aaron at bavariati.org  Thu Dec 21 12:37:55 2006

	<fn4r54x3rd.ln2@ursa-major.ursine.ca>
Message-ID: <20061221203755.GB14174@syrinxpc.com>

On Thu, Dec 21, 2006 at 11:43:11AM -0800, Paul Johnson wrote:
> What's wrong with dd if=/dev/random of=/dev/hda?

/dev/random runs out of entropy quickly.  Best use /dev/urandom instead.

I remember hearing an ex-Sequent dev griping about how slow the Linux
random-number device was.  "How hard can it be to generate random
numbers?  I can write a program to generate them faster myself!"

When I tried to explain the difference between real and pseudo-random
numbers, his eyes glazed over.  I don't think he even heard the
important bit, "/dev/urandom is faster" before he wandered off to find
someone more gripe-friendly.

> > My mother's computer is exhibiting a strange problem, and I'm not
> > familiar enough with Kubuntu/Debian to figure it out. Whenever the PC is
> > powered down, it comes back up with the system clock being hours off. I'm
> > guessing what's happening is the RTC is being written UTC at shutdown and
> > being read as local at startup.
>
> Yep. In Kubuntu:
> K Menu ->
> System Settings ->
> System Administration section Date & Time ->
> Enter administrator mode with the button on the lower right ->
> Select timezone ->
> Close
>
> Pretty easy

Checked that, it's currently set to Los Angeles which is correct.


> How old is the computer? ?Lost clock setting is usually a sign of a
> dying battery.
>
> - tony

The computer is about 4 months old, no other signs of failing battery.

I'm 99% sure this is a problem with however it's setting the system clock from 
the hardware clock. On other distros you can configure someplace on the 
system whether the RTC stores UTC or local time, and the hwclock call at 
shutdown will take that into account. I'll check the BIOS later to see if 
it's being written incorrectly or read incorrectly.

I read the debian README about the hwclock init script and it doesn't say 
anything about these settings.

Thanks for the ideas so far.

- Aaron

On Thu, 2006-12-21 at 12:38 -0800, Russ Gilman-Hunt wrote:

> Just out of curiosity, what makes urandom so much better, and why don't
> we just link /dev/random to /dev/urandom? 

JFWPI:

http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki//dev/random

In short, /dev/urandom contains less entropy (it's "less random")
than /dev/random, but /dev/urandom will produce w/o blocking,
whereas /dev/random will block (will seem to stall/take much longer to
generate a given given number of random bytes).

Wil
-- 
Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc>
http://nakedape.cc

Subject: [PLUG] Re: WipeInfo Equivalent
In-Reply-To: <20061221123808.6bdc6838@badger.had1.or.comcast.net.>

	<20061221203755.GB14174@syrinxpc.com>
	<20061221123808.6bdc6838@badger.had1.or.comcast.net.>
Message-ID: <20061221224308.GE14174@syrinxpc.com>

On Thu, Dec 21, 2006 at 12:38:08PM -0800, Russ Gilman-Hunt wrote:
> Just out of curiosity, what makes urandom so much better, and why don't
> we just link /dev/random to /dev/urandom? 

/dev/random is a source of honest-to-goodness truly random numbers,
carefully collected from the wild.  It is impossible to guess what the
next number will be based on the previous ones.

/dev/urandom is a source of pseudo-random numbers, which are generated
by an algorithm.  Algorithms always do the same thing, so it's possible
to guess what the next number will be based on the previous ones.

Problem with /dev/random is, there's only so much entropy (randomness) 
available at a time*.  So it can't generate numbers very fast.

So you use /dev/urandom if you need a whole lot of somewhat-random
numbers real fast, and you use /dev/random if you want a handful of
truly-random numbers.  Quantity vs. quality, y'see.

Causing eyes to glaze over since I could talk,
  Aaron

* see: http://www.number.ch/wiki/index.php/LinuxEntropySources


From ehem at m5p.com  Thu Dec 21 15:12:04 2006
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Thu, 21 Dec 2006 15:12:04 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Running fsck
In-Reply-To: <20061221133607.319F64439@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200612212312.kBLNC5ue066163@m5p.com>

>From: Keith Lofstrom <keithl at kl-ic.com>
> On Wed, Dec 20, 2006 at 04:21:56PM -0800, John Jason Jordan wrote:
> > 
> > Also, how does one run fsck? You can't run it on a mounted partition,
> > right? But I have to be booted into Linux to use it. Is the normal
> > procedure to use a live CD?
> 
> There are two standard ways.  They involve a text window, as root.
> 
> 1)  Bring up a text window.  change user to root:
>   [john]$  su - root
>   Password:  YourRootPasswordHere
>   [root]#  touch /forcefsck
>   [root]#  reboot            (or just do it with the GUI)

This is the "standard" way, it will work on most flavors of Unix (haven't
seen one where it doesn't work, but just in case there was someone insane
out there). The advanced method is to remount the filesystem read-only
(mount <mount point|device> [-n] -o ro,remount), and then run fsck. This
is what creating /forcefsck or shutdown -F cause to happen behind the
scenes. The real trick is to make / and /usr read-only, and which point
the only concern is /var (but which the system can get most of the way up
without). Having the filesystems read-only also tends to avoid damage
being done in the first place.

> Oh, and do backups.  See "www.dirvish.org" for my favorite method ...

Indeed, backups, backups, and just in case you didn't hear it the first
time, backups. I prefer `dump` (SysV-style!) once you figure out the
syntax, it is a very effective backup tool.

From ron at Opus1.COM  Thu Dec 21 22:50:24 2006
From: ron at Opus1.COM (Ronald Chmara)
Date: Thu, 21 Dec 2006 22:50:24 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Confusing php warning...
In-Reply-To: <1166672514.11149.1.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
References: <1166672514.11149.1.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <C7541DA9-794E-4F48-9E4D-0A3BB845940C@opus1.com>

On Dec 20, 2006, at 7:41 PM, someone wrote:
> "As of PHP 4.3.0, the index generation behaviour described above has
> changed. Now, if you append to an array in which the current  
> maximum key
> is negative, then the next key created will be zero (0). Before,  
> the new
> index would have been set to the largest existing key + 1, the same as
> positive indices are."
>
> I don't understand this warning.  How can a key be negative???

IIRC: (not tested for syntax)
$foo[-200]= 'bar'; //array value of a string, with a key value of  
*negative* 200 numeric integer
$foo["$i--"]= 'bar'; //array value of a string, with a key value of  
uhm, who knows, it's a parsed string value, decremented, if the  
string is 0/false, foo123/123, or $i=$foo-$baz, do fun and exciting  
different things.

>   Are keys
> and indices the same thing?

In programmer-space:
Keys are usually user-made, and can be user controlled. Indicies are  
auto-generated, but can be user-manipulated. How the auto-generated  
keys were *created* has changed to alter negative values.

>   Aren't you supposed
> to specify the key when appending to a php array?

Nope. (shouldn't computers do that work? :P)

$foo[]= "baz"; // next index number (aka key) for this array element  
is being generated by php

This comes in quite handy, actually, when dealing with http forms and  
an unknown number of array values. You can submit a whole group of  
values, and let PHP auto-build the array, or alternately, build  
arrays of unknown sizes without caring to specify keys for 10, or  
10000000, key=>value pairs.

It can get real fun when you see a line like:
$$foo["$$key--"]= $$value; //decrement a variable variable key, and  
assign a value to that variable variable array element with the  
variable variable key value, and then assign it to an variable  
variable array

>   Why this behavior change?

/me cringes at the thought of explaining PHP politics in changes like  
this

I'll guess at it, but only because I've been in PHP-land a long time.

The really short answer would be that negative values for things are  
often seen as, well, "bad".

The slightly longer answer is the PHP team spends a massive amount of  
time trying to cut down on the WTF factor, so adding an element to an  
array should add to something. Since many PHP users (such as you) are  
not in the habit of thinking that, oh, an array can have negative  
keys, might as well move stuff to zero-based arrays, so adding an  
element puts something into *positive* integer space, to avoid false  
<0 (false, heh) errors.

(Something I tell perl hackers moving into PHP: forget everything you  
know about groups of variables, PHP is totally different. I have to  
repeat this often, FWIW.)

The much longer answer likely involves at least a good 200-300 email  
messages on the PHP lists, where simple useOfCamelCase (in the *docs) 
* discussions took months to resolve, so +/- int wars in arrays might  
be, well..... ugly.

-Bop
(aka ronabop at php.net)

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Fri Dec 22 07:19:49 2006
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Fri, 22 Dec 2006 07:19:49 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Wide-screen monitors
In-Reply-To: <20061222073553.6618.qmail@web50601.mail.yahoo.com>
References: <20061222073553.6618.qmail@web50601.mail.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <20061222151949.GA3086@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Thu, Dec 21, 2006 at 11:35:53PM -0800, Robert Kopp wrote:
> I am thinking about getting a wide-screen TFT monitor, probably
> with a resolution of 1680 x 1050 (WUXGA). I have heard > conflicting
> reports as to the adaptability of Linux to such a monitor.  Is it
> likely that it would work with native resolution of the monitor?
> Are there any potential pitfalls? 

Check the archives; we had quite a discussion about this about a month
ago.  The quick answer is that while some distros combined with some
video boards might not automagically recognize an "odd" size like
1680x1050, you can easily talk to that or any other flat screen
resolution if you use the right video driver and fiddle with the X
configuration.  Chances are, though, it will "just work".

You will probably want to buy a DVI video card if you do not already
have one, and that might be slightly tricky - the addon video cards
are mostly for gamers, and burn a lot of power and cost a bunch.  To
get something lower power, I bought four used boards from the store
at Free Geek and tested them until I found the best one.

But again, check the archives, we discussed this.

Keith

Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0612220821280.10204@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Fri, 22 Dec 2006, Tim Garton wrote:

> I am wondering if you think it wiser to have one server act as the
> database server for all these apps and the other server act as the
> webserver for them all, or to break it out so that each server acts as a
> database and webserver and serves up half the apps?

Tim,

   What situation are you trying to avoid?

   From your description, all applications are mission critical. If that's
correct, then the company cannot afford to lose any single piece for more
than a coffee break. In this situation it makes more sense to replicate
everything on a separate box so if one goes down the other remains up and
folks are still productive.

   If that's not the case, but you're trying to spread a heavy load for beter
rsponse times, then partitioning the tools between the two systems makes
more sense.

Rich

> If the servers are equivalent and can support the entire group of apps
> consider
> putting everything on both boxes.  Use one for production, the other as a
> hot
> spare or testing/dev box.

From heinlein at madboa.com  Fri Dec 22 08:53:33 2006
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Fri, 22 Dec 2006 08:53:33 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Separating out MySQL and Apache to different boxes?
In-Reply-To: <8fcf3b560612220814u3a0b4cefhf2110675bbe56096@mail.gmail.com>
References: <8fcf3b560612220814u3a0b4cefhf2110675bbe56096@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.OSX.4.64.0612220844540.29305@arrowhead.galois.com>

On Fri, 22 Dec 2006, Tim Garton wrote:

>    I'm curious as to people's opinions about the following:  My 
> company just purchased two new servers to consolidate a bunch of 
> services we currently have running on miscellaneous boxes (bugzilla, 
> wiki, request tracker, xplanner, dev and qa versions of our app, 
> etc.).  I am wondering if you think it wiser to have one server act 
> as the database server for all these apps and the other server act 
> as the webserver for them all, or to break it out so that each 
> server acts as a database and webserver and serves up half the apps?

You haven't given us enough information to be good back-seat drivers. 
:-)

* How resource-intensive are your Apache applications? Bugzilla, RT,
  and wiki software needn't be a huge burden if configured correctly
  -- but they can chew lots of CPU if they get busy.

* How big/busy are your databases?

* How robust and fast is the network connecting the boxes?

* How much downtime can you stand?

If a single box has the horsepower and I/O bandwidth to handle it all, 
and uptime is a Real Need, I'd consolidate onto one server, making the 
second a hot spare using database replication and, perhaps, heartbeat 
or something similar.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com


From amerine at gmail.com  Fri Dec 22 09:17:01 2006

Is there an entry for root in your /etc/aliases file?

With pflogsumm I have found it easier to just mail the results from
the cron job. For example here is the line from one of my  mail
servers:

45 0 * * * /usr/sbin/pflogsumm -d yesterday -u 100 -h 100
/var/log/mail.log.0 |/usr/bin/mail -s "newberry - Postfix daily mail
summary" serveradmins at amerine.net

I rotate the log at 12:25 am and have pflogsumm work on teh previous
days log at 12:45am. I just pipe the results to "mail".

-Mark

On Thu, Dec 21, 2006 at 11:35:53PM -0800, Robert Kopp wrote:
> I am thinking about getting a wide-screen TFT monitor, probably
> with a resolution of 1680 x 1050 (WUXGA). I have heard > conflicting
> reports as to the adaptability of Linux to such a monitor.  Is it
> likely that it would work with native resolution of the monitor?
> Are there any potential pitfalls? 

Check the archives; we had quite a discussion about this about a month
ago.  The quick answer is that while some distros combined with some
video boards might not automagically recognize an "odd" size like
1680x1050, you can easily talk to that or any other flat screen
resolution if you use the right video driver and fiddle with the X
configuration.  Chances are, though, it will "just work".





I have an Nvidia card. They provide their own Linux drivers and instructions. If it Just Works, I'll be done; otherwise, including a ModeLine in xorg.conf often works. Let's first see if it Just Works...But

Modeline "1680x1050" 146.2 1680 1784 1960 2240 1050 1053 1059 1089

From ehem at m5p.com  Fri Dec 22 11:36:33 2006
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Fri, 22 Dec 2006 11:36:33 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Re: WipeInfo Equivalent
In-Reply-To: <20061222144214.E618D445F@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200612221936.kBMJaX65076692@m5p.com>

>From: Aaron Burt <aaron at bavariati.org>
> On Thu, Dec 21, 2006 at 12:38:08PM -0800, Russ Gilman-Hunt wrote:
> > Just out of curiosity, what makes urandom so much better, and why don't
> > we just link /dev/random to /dev/urandom? 
> 
> /dev/random is a source of honest-to-goodness truly random numbers,
> carefully collected from the wild.  It is impossible to guess what the
> next number will be based on the previous ones.
> 
> /dev/urandom is a source of pseudo-random numbers, which are generated
> by an algorithm.  Algorithms always do the same thing, so it's possible
> to guess what the next number will be based on the previous ones.

Closer but not quite. /dev/urandom will also give you random numbers, but
will always give data back. In order to fill in for true random numbers,
urandom will also use pseudo-random numbers, but if a sufficient amount
of randomness is available urandom will give true random numbers. urandom
isn't pure psuedo-random, just you might merely get pseudo-random numbers
instead of truely random ones.

> So you use /dev/urandom if you need a whole lot of somewhat-random
> numbers real fast, and you use /dev/random if you want a handful of
> truly-random numbers.  Quantity vs. quality, y'see.

Got it here. You use /dev/random for things like public/private key pair
generation, where quality is much more important. /dev/urandom is used
for session keys where randomness is important, but getting some
randomness is much more important.

I'm not a web-app developer nor a dba, but why should web-developers
need to be db experts?

For most of the web applications that are simple and useful, things such
as adodb (or other language specific db api's) and a wiki full of
examples should provide enough to keep a developer creating a useful app.

If the developer is code competent, I would like to see more time spent
on security, UI design, and unit testing.

Now, if you're running critical applications on allocated CPU time on
WOPR.. maybe you need to be competent in all aspects of your
application.  Does apache even run on WOPR?[1] ;)



[1] Probably.. but you will need NetBSD first.

> examples should provide enough to keep a developer creating a useful app.

I don't think the interface is relevant to Mr' Burt's comment. He's
suggesting that if you're writing code that cares about the database,
YOU should know something about what the database is doing with your
tables and queries.

For most web apps that are simple and useful, each developer is probably
creating their own tables and crafting their own queries, right? Don't
you think it's reasonable to expect them to have a clue how the database
works?

> If the developer is code competent, I would like to see more time spent
> on security, UI design, and unit testing.

You might conside db design as part of the UI.  It has a HUGE effect on
response time in a busy system. 

> Now, if you're running critical applications on allocated CPU time on
> WOPR.. maybe you need to be competent in all aspects of your
> application.  Does apache even run on WOPR?[1] ;)

I guess competence is underrated. I would rather be competent in
everything for which I bill. Maybe that's just me.

On Fri, 22 Dec 2006 15:53:08 -0800
Kris <krisa at subtend.net> wrote:
(snip)

> I'm not a web-app developer nor a dba, but why should web-developers
> need to be db experts?
(snip)

Well, to my shame, I brought down a website just this past week due to
a mistake with the query.

I took a query that looked like this "select * from blah where (this or
that or the-other)" and changed it to "select * from blah where (that
or the-other)". "this" and "that" were semi-redundant
("field<linux_epoch_of_now OR field == 1"). The table had about
1,500,000 rows. With removing this OR clause, I changed which index the
query used from one with a cardinality of about 35000 to one with a
cardinality of about 1,500,000. The search slowed down to about 200
seconds per search, which caused the entire website to crash; they get
about 280,000 unique visitors daily, and I just overloaded the system.
All the web servers using the database went off-line, all the
replication slaves went off-line, and I couldn't back up my change
because the database was off-line (no authentication ... etc.)

I know, now, _why_ what I did broke the system. But before then, it was
"just remove one semi-redundant subclause in the OR clause."  Web
developer; not so much a database expert, apparently, though I'm pretty
good in general with construction of SQL statements. 

My point is- web developing without being a db expert ... can cause
problems that would not otherwise crop up. 

-- 
Russ Gilman-Hunt
http://www.arghwebworks.com/
gilmanhunt at comcast.net

is that you can fix most database problems with hardware. And it's true,
you can. But it's not elegant, and (cue DHH flying into a rage) it
doesn't scale.

OK, I'll go with the team approach, up to a point. In my experience, the
best developers know enough about everything they're working with to
make good decisions, and to improve on the sub-optimal ones. 

One of the best programmers I ever worked with said that programmers do
two things: debugging and enbugging. Enbugging is relatively easy, and
does not require much knowledge outside of the syntax of your chosen
language (this is clearly a ridiculous exaggeration, OK?).  Debugging,
on the other hand, requires deep knowledge of almost everything your
code touches. Ergo a programmer can be more productive with a deeper
understanding of databases, if the app touches a database.

On Dec 22, 2006, at 3:53 PM, Kris wrote:
> Aaron Burt wrote:
>> OTOH, web-apps usually use RDBMSes for storage, and it'd be silly for
>> someone writing a web-app to not understand the basics.  Mind, they
>> won't learn it in university (too practical) and they won't learn  
>> it on
>> the job unless they're forced to.
> I'm not a web-app developer nor a dba, but why should web-developers
> need to be db experts?

I wrote a long rant, then deleted it, on this topic.

Suffice to say that I believe that people who take on obligations,  
that can affect other people, should also assume a level of  
*responsibility* to those other people who they can affect.

> For most of the web applications that are simple and useful, things  
> such
> as adodb (or other language specific db api's) and a wiki full of
> examples should provide enough to keep a developer creating a  
> useful app.

Useful for a solo demo/test site, yes.

Add a few million users, or a few million records, and I would guess  
that a good 99% of the tutorial examples and wikis in existence are  
not only wrong for such a scenario, but they are actually wrong in  
such a way as to significantly impact the material lives of other  
people. Medications lost, equipment destroyed, sex offenders let out  
of prison after a one day sentence... It's Y2K every day when people  
seriously consider the idea that "managing data is unimportant in  
writing programs that, well, deal with data".

> If the developer is code competent, I would like to see more time  
> spent
> on security, UI design, and unit testing.

DB request overload: DoS, Security
DB fragility: DoS, Security
DB table confusion: Bad UI to work with the tables
Bad DB table design: Bad UI to work with the tables
DB design not managed: Unit tests provide incomprehensible results

If the developer is code competent in *anything* that involves piles  
of data, they absolutely need to know DB work. The DB could be flat  
files, a RDBMS, whatever, but efficient data management is often a  
good way of separating the app coders from the desktop technicians  
who fix the "drink tray".

A fairly simple test is to ask 20 different programmer applicants to  
design an address book DB. You *will* likely be amazed at the  
variance in the results.

> Now, if you're running critical applications on allocated CPU time on
> WOPR.. maybe you need to be competent in all aspects of your
> application.  Does apache even run on WOPR?[1] ;)
> [1] Probably.. but you will need NetBSD first.

NetBSD does not like to play games. :)

Does PLUG have a site where I can play a nice game of chess?

-Bop

On Fri, Dec 22, 2006 at 03:53:08PM -0800, Kris wrote:
> Aaron Burt wrote:
> > OTOH, web-apps usually use RDBMSes for storage, and it'd be silly for
> > someone writing a web-app to not understand the basics.  Mind, they
> > won't learn it in university (too practical) and they won't learn it on
> > the job unless they're forced to.
> 
> I'm not a web-app developer nor a dba, but why should web-developers
> need to be db experts?

They just have to understand the basics.  Practically speaking, most
web-apps are just front-ends to a relational database.  And unless you
understand how a relational database works and what it's good at doing,
you can't design an application that makes effective use of it.

The DB *expert* is useful when your WikiBaySter/Web-2.0/Folksonomy site
takes off and you can't upgrade fast enough to keep up with traffic.

Web-servers are just glorified FEPs,
  Aaron

Subject 'subject', Account: 'pop3.hevanet.com', Server: 'mail.hevanet.com', Protocol: SMTP, Server Response: '550 5.7.1 <koppr at hevanet.com>... Relaying denied', Port: 25, Secure(SSL): No, Server Error: 550, Error Number: 0x800CCC79"

I have a comcast email account (expired for a year+) that still
receives mail, but I cannot use it to send mail.  I think it's common
practice for ISPs to terminate accounts in this fashion.

If you cannot send mail through the account via a telnet session or
other reasonably clinical means, you're probably out of luck.  At this
point, your choices are to secure an email account with some other
provider, or set up your own email server.

Some possibilities for email account providers:

     - web hosting service
     - private linux geek who is set up already
     - yahoo, gmail

To run your own email server, you will need a domain name and a static
IP address.  The static IP address must be obtained from Qwest, and
your monthly fee will go up.

If you use Qwest DSL, your modem is probably an Actiontec modem/home
router.  These stink for the home linux nerd who wants to run his own
firewall and stuff.  There are other modem options, but you'll have to
buy the modem yourself (and probably support it yourself too).  I'd
bet there is good modem choice advice to be found on this very list.

I found setting up my own email server to be a fairly extensive
project.  But it was fun and rewarding, and I became proficient in
many areas as a result of my efforts: firewalls, DNS name servers,
mail servers, imap servers, SSL and SASL, to name some.  For the true
linux nerd, setting up a mail server is like playing arpeggios on the
violin; an essential technique-building exercise.  I wouldn't hesitate
to dive into the task, but I'd plan a month of Sundays worth of time.

I went with postfix and courier-imap for my mail server, with a
postgresql backend, and postfixadmin for account administration.  Once
I finally got it all working, it has been running like a champ.
postfixadmin makes account administration a breeze.  It's just a web
GUI that wraps SQL queries.  You can get the same result by issuing
the right SQL queries from a psql shell, but it's more laborious that
way.

Being a gentoo user, I went to google and typed in "gentoo wiki
postfix postgresql" and got these links:

     http://gentoo-wiki.com/HOWTO_Email:_A_Complete_Virtual_System_-_Postfix_to_Postgres
     http://gentoo-wiki.com/Postfix

Carlos Konstanski

Robert-
Its because most ISP's block outbound email that isn't from their same
netblock, so when Hevanet's server's receive your request and see that its
from a Qwest IP address they block it based on that, blame spammers. You may
want to ping Hevanet support and see if they have any authenticated SMTP
servers available which would then let you send email after you have sent
your username and password and its been verified by Hevanet's SMTP server.

Drew-

My hunch, and it's only that, is that Fedora Core 5 would be the 
closest jumping-off point. FC6, nice as it is, is a bit too new; 
there's no way its current package set could have gone through all the 
vendor testing necessary for RHEL certification.

I suspect they'd know pretty intimately.

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Tue Dec 26 12:35:12 2006
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (someone)
Date: Tue, 26 Dec 2006 12:35:12 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] E-mail server?
In-Reply-To: <4590A6C9.9070307@telestream.com>
References: <20061225201358.34078.qmail@web50613.mail.yahoo.com>
	<e2aeccd60612251719g7712b69cq9ed5a831e5319a87@mail.gmail.com>
	<4590A6C9.9070307@telestream.com>
Message-ID: <1167165312.19758.47.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>

On Mon, 2006-12-25 at 20:36 -0800, m0gely wrote:
> drew wymore wrote:
> > Robert-
> > Its because most ISP's block outbound email that isn't from their same
> > netblock, so when Hevanet's server's receive your request and see that its
> > from a Qwest IP address they block it based on that, blame spammers. You 
> > may
> > want to ping Hevanet support and see if they have any authenticated SMTP
> > servers available which would then let you send email after you have sent
> > your username and password and its been verified by Hevanet's SMTP server.
> 
> Or just use Qwest's SMTP for sending email.

Because of the spam issue, I question the idea of using Qwest's smtp
server to send email and another ISP's email account to receive it.
Because of SPF, sender protection framework, someone could say that
heavanet accounts aren't hosted by Qwest and reject email from you 
based on that.  If you can keep your heavanet account open and forward
email from your qwest account to it, then as long as you use Qwest
credentials with your qwest account you should be fine.  Downside, if
you want people to send to a qwest email account you will have 
to inform them that your email address has changed.  If you want from
qwest with a reply address of heavanet, you'll have to set that up
in whichever mail user agent you use.  This means you'll have to
think, "I'm doing something different," if you use any qwest supplied
web based email interface.  If you want to make sure the reply-to
doesn't go to the qwest account...

Getting a static ip and domain name to do an email site 
allows one to keep the same email addresses no matter which 
DSL provider is chosen.  Unfortunately, Comcast is out for 
doing your own domain unless you want to mess with dynamic 
dns.  Too bad there isn't a competitor to Comcast offering 
static subnets at a reasonable price.  I don't buy Comcast's, 
"putting everyone on dynamic ip addresses makes managing our 
network easier," comments by the way.

If you go to http://register.com/, 
you are looking at $35/year adjusted down for people who pay for 
more than one year at a time.  I would imagine you are going to
pay at least $5 per ip for static ip addresses.  There is also
probably going to be a bridging fee because the Actiontec will
need to be programmed to stop doing NAT and reprogrammed to 
silently pass packets across qwest's network onto yours and 
vice versa.

What I am curious about, can you dynamically map a domain name to
a dynamically assigned ip address?  It's safer to have someone
intercept your email for you in this case and you grab it 
later via pop.  The problem with trying to dynamically change 
the dns address of your email server is the delay for those 
changes to propagate across the Internet.  Stale information 
could cause email to be routed wrong and get lost.  Setting 
up SPF would be difficult, I guess you'd have to know the
subnet you are being assigned out of and allow every single 
ip address in it.  The latter isn't a very secure plan.  In
essence, you are better off going with a hosting service if 
you don't have your own static subnet on the Internet.

If I were to start my own ISP, I wouldn't put anyone on random
ip addresses.  Everybody would be static, but I'd offer dhcp
service and a web interface to allow customers to configure 
it that want to use it.  I'd also provide dns service standard 
for those who don't want to do dns on their own servers.  The
trick, keep intentional ip misconfigurations from interfering
with other customers.  Instead of an email service, customers
do their own email servers and they can forward to a filtering
server that I provide for them if they want it.

     Michael C. Robinson

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Wed Dec 27 15:13:44 2006
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Wed, 27 Dec 2006 15:13:44 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Saving mailing lists with mutt
Message-ID: <20061227231344.GA19197@gate.kl-ic.com>


Handy tricks for mailing lists and mutt:

Some mailing lists rewrite the From: in the mailing header to show
the list, while others indicate the show the list in the Sender:
line and leave the original From: intact.  

I use mutt, and often save interesting mailings from lists in 
appropriately named mail folders.  For example, I save most things
from pdx-pm-list in the "perl" folder.  However, I get the mails as
individual files with From: lines of the authors, not the list, which
makes saving more interesting.

For lists that rewrite headers (like the plug lists), I can use a
simple save-hook line in the .muttrc file, like so:

save-hook "lists.pdxlinux.org" +plug

This only looks by default at the From: line.  However, I can make
it look at the whole header with:

save-hook "~h pdx-pm-list" +perl

... and that will apply the save-hook to any file that has pdx-pm-list
anywhere in the header.  If that string appears in a subject line, I
may have to redirect the save manually, but chances are I want to save
it in perl anyway.

I suppose I could get fancy with rewrite rules in procmail, and save
all plug files to a mail folder on the way in, but some things aren't
worth saving.

Keith

Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

Red Hat does mention FC6 as the source for RHEL5 on their own website;
in this case, the conservatism is reflected by the fact that RHEL5 is
more than 6 months late (compared to their target 18 month version
cycle for Enterprise).  

It turns out that the RHEL clone that I use, Scientific Linux, has a
version 5 alpha on their site (as a heap of loose RPMs, they haven't
built a distro yet).  I think what I will do is load S.L. version 4.4,
then attempt to upgrade it with those RPMs

And why am do I use Scientific Linux?  Compatability and support. 
Some of my CAD apps and other proprietary (sadly) stuff want to
install on a major distro, and all support RHEL.  S.L. made a lot of
improvements for the technical and scientific user.  S.L. is run on
the huge compute clusters at various government and academic labs, and
they don't change them much after the initial install, beyond bug-fixes
and security updates.  That fits my needs well.  Fermilabs has four
full time staff working on S.L., with the goal of supporting and
updating all their versions into the very distant future, much longer
than Red Hat.  So even though I have no problems with paying for RHEL,
it would reach end-of-life too soon, before I want to upgrade.  

Keith

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Wed Dec 27 16:35:41 2006
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Wed, 27 Dec 2006 16:35:41 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] bugs in Vista betas
Message-ID: <20061228003541.GC19711@gate.kl-ic.com>

A friend wrote: 
> Beta Testers are finding flaws in Microshaft's Vista.

I wouldn't fault Microsoft for this, unless they react badly.  Finding
flaws is what beta test is for ("beta than nothing" as they say). 
My real worry is that the bad publicity will cause them to shoot the
messenger, scale back their beta testing process, and postpone the 
outside bug-finding process until after major release.  All new
software contains flaws that are only findable by outsiders.  Eric
Raymond says, "with enough eyes, all bugs are shallow." Every bug
found now is one less that could torment tens of millions of users
in the future.  We should celebrate these flaws.

So while there are a lot of things I don't like about Microsoft, bugs
found in betas are not one of them.  Open source software has many more
bugs on initial release, and relies on community involvement to find
them.   Over time, the bugs are found and the software becomes really
robust and bug-free.  I would like to see Microsoft release betas much
sooner, and let the world find the flaws sooner, before the flaws
become cast in stone.

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

 > available options is the easiest starting point.
 >
 > Keith
 >
 >

I believe RHEL4 --> RHEL5 is a supported upgrade path.  Considering that 
CentOS is a rebrand of Red Hat, I would expect the same upgrade path to 
work.

RHEL5 Beta 2 is based on FC6.  Mixing FC and RHEL often works, but can 
also cause problems so I would not recommend mixing them if you are 
considering upgrading it.  Unless you really want/need virtualization, 
RHEL4 works well.

I've been running RHEL5 Beta 2 and adding in FC6 packages via yum on an 
as needed basis for packages not in RHEL5 and it works fine.

John

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Wed Dec 27 18:13:04 2006
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Wed, 27 Dec 2006 18:13:04 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Phone/PDA Recommendations

On Wed, 27 Dec 2006, Charles Sliger wrote:

> I'm looking for insights on phone/pda combo's.

Charles,

   For almost six years now (anniversary in a couple of weeks) I've been
happily using my Kyocera QCP-6035. It's a PalmOS PDA (I think equivalent to
the Palm III) and a cell phone. It will also do text messaging and more, but
I don't have any need for that stuff.

   There are newer models (color screens and I don't know what else). It has
worked flawlessly for me all this time; it lives in a full leather jacket
and has survived being dropped on the ground a half-dozen or so times. I've
upgraded the firmware two or three times and the local Verizon store.
There's also the Palm Treo but I've never used anything but my trusty ol'
6035.

   Since about 1998 I've used Judd Montgomery's jpilot desktop. It works with
the standard pilot link library. It's worked for every PalmOS PDA we've had
from the Personal through the Handspring Visor Pro; serial and USB. Look at
<http://www.jpilot.org/>. It's run on Red Hat (4.1-7.3) and Slackware
(8.1-11.0). Judd's a great guy and has done a terrific job with this tool.

   I won't buy any device that runs any flavor of Microsoft; that's my
preference. The PalmOS works just fine for me. There are several linux-based
PDAs, but I don't know if any of them include a cell phone.

Rich

From luke at xifos.org  Wed Dec 27 20:15:20 2006
From: luke at xifos.org (Luke Eckley)
Date: Wed, 27 Dec 2006 20:15:20 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Web Server
In-Reply-To: <e89b36dd0612271839i64025289tf9e679fe93231a50@mail.gmail.com>
References: <e89b36dd0612271607i6bf6ec17wddf7d35502c24348@mail.gmail.com>
	<ca5165530612271734j1f43010frc663a4f617e1e790@mail.gmail.com>
	<e89b36dd0612271839i64025289tf9e679fe93231a50@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <20061228041520.GA25668@xifos.org>

On Wed, Dec 27, 2006 at 06:39:07PM -0800, randy.rowlette at gmail.com wrote:
> I have commented out those lines and have been able to put an index.htm file
> in the directory which will show up as an index. After clicking on the index
> icon my text shows. But I am unable to get straight to the web page without
> the index and I think that may have something to do with being a subdomain
> as the place I have my domain name will not allow me to point that one to my
> server. Obviously time for a change.
> 
> Thanks for the info.

What file extension are you using? .xhtml? If so you probably need to
edit your httpd.conf file and add index.xhtml so it will be the first
file apache will look for. Just add it right after DirectoryIndex and
leave what ever other files are listed there. It might look like this:

DirectoryIndex index.xhtml index.html index.htm index.php

Luke Eckley
http://luke.xifos.org

From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Thu Dec 28 09:13:07 2006
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Thu, 28 Dec 2006 09:13:07 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Phone/PDA Recommendations
In-Reply-To: <B0028721931@bct-mail.bctonline.com>
References: <B0028721931@bct-mail.bctonline.com>
Message-ID: <1167325987.18226.14.camel@willow.odshp.com>

On Wed, 2006-12-27 at 16:46 -0800, Charles Sliger wrote:
> I'm looking for insights on phone/pda combo's.
> 
> I like the look of the ones that open up and have a full keyboard.
> 
> I use MS/Outlook now but I'd like to transition to something else like
> Thunderbird.
> 
> I've gotten the impression that syncing with something other than Outlook is
> rare.
> 
> I want to find a device that can sync with my desktop for things like
> schedules, contacts, notes, etc.
> 
> I've had problems in the past getting repeating appointments and tasks to
> sync.
> 
> Any insights appreciated.

A Treo is the way to go.  I have the same Kyocera that Rich mentions,
but you can no longer get new service for it due to its lack of E911 or
somesuch.  But I don't miss it; my Treo 650 is so much nicer--the color
screen is much more usable in low-light and much higher res, so Google
Maps[1] is actually usable.  It has enough CPU power and an SD expansion
slot so it's also quite capable as a music player.

And yes, it syncs with Linux.  Kinda.  The data formats for calendar,
contacts, etc are well-supported, since they haven't changed much over
the years.  Actually syncing with the stock 'visor' kernel module is
spotty; it works sometimes and sometimes it doesn't.  I don't think it's
seen much love lately.  pilot-link[2] has finally had a new release
after 3(!!!) years, which includes support for talking directly to the
device via libusb, instead of having its access mediated by a kernel
module.  From the little testing I've done, it's much faster and works
reliably, but currently requires to be run as root to be able to
open /proc/bus/usb/* as it needs.


1. http://www.google.com/gmm/treo
2. http://www.pilot-link.org/

Wil
-- 
Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc>
http://nakedape.cc

From xrdawson at gmail.com  Thu Dec 28 09:40:04 2006
From: xrdawson at gmail.com (Chris Dawson)
Date: Thu, 28 Dec 2006 09:40:04 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Phone/PDA Recommendations
In-Reply-To: <1167325987.18226.14.camel@willow.odshp.com>

Message-ID: <659b9ea30612280940w5c6e183boadb3062cd5d0a089@mail.gmail.com>

I'll second the Treo recommendation.  I love my Treo.  I cannot live
without it.  It has decent support on Linux, which is to say, the best
that you will find with any other cell/pda combo right now.  PalmOS
has the largest variety of apps.  Lastly, and this should not be
understated, they have the best interface for a pda hands-down.  I
switched temporarily to a Blackberry, but couldn't use it due to zero
linux support, but also because I could not get used to the interfaces
for entering data.  Once you start entering data into your Treo, you
will not be able to use other interfaces because they are inferior,
IMHO.  This goes for the PocketPC Treo; I'd stay away from that.

Chris

The Treo 650 has the new Contacts and Calendar PIM applications which
changed the format of the databases.  Syncing them with Evolution or
other PIMs no longer works.  The pilot-link guys are working on adding
support for the new databases. There are compatibility databases for
the old-style programs but those sync as empty databases.

I use pilot-link to backup my Treo and to install programs.  Sending
files over Bluetooth works well.  Mounting the SD card over USB also
works.  My impression is that Windows Mobile does not work nearly as
well with Linux or Mac.

 - Ian

From m0gely at telestream.com  Thu Dec 28 18:58:06 2006
From: m0gely at telestream.com (m0gely)
Date: Thu, 28 Dec 2006 18:58:06 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] E-mail server?
In-Reply-To: <1167165312.19758.47.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
References: <20061225201358.34078.qmail@web50613.mail.yahoo.com>	<e2aeccd60612251719g7712b69cq9ed5a831e5319a87@mail.gmail.com>	<4590A6C9.9070307@telestream.com>
	<1167165312.19758.47.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <4594843E.1000103@telestream.com>

someone wrote:

> Because of the spam issue, I question the idea of using Qwest's smtp
> server to send email and another ISP's email account to receive it.

Maybe I'm speaking ignorantly here, but that's exactly how I solved my 
problems with AOL, Yahoo and Hotmail.  My mail was getting rejected or 
automatically dumped into the spam bin for those providers when I tried 
to send direct from my own server.  My reverse DNS and PTR records are 
fine.  dnsreports.com say everything is good on my end, yet nothing 
would fix the actual problem, but that.  I set my mail server to forward 
to my upstream providers SMTP and all is now well.

> Too bad there isn't a competitor to Comcast offering 
> static subnets at a reasonable price.

Spiritone.com (happy customer, don't work there)

> I don't buy Comcast's, 
> "putting everyone on dynamic ip addresses makes managing our 
> network easier," comments by the way.

Are you kidding?  It's much easier to push out mass IP that way.  I 
would like to know how static is easier to manage if you can point me in 
a direction.

> I would imagine you are going to
> pay at least $5 per ip for static ip addresses.  There is also
> probably going to be a bridging fee because the Actiontec will
> need to be programmed to stop doing NAT and reprogrammed to 
> silently pass packets across qwest's network onto yours and 
> vice versa.

Bah, as I said; spiritone.com :)  Business accounts get four static 
IP's.  Consumer accounts get one.

> If I were to start my own ISP, I wouldn't put anyone on random
> ip addresses.  Everybody would be static, but I'd offer dhcp
> service and a web interface to allow customers to configure 
> it that want to use it.

All I can say is if you really think about it, and think about the 
future, dynamic IP's are a nice thing to have.  I'm not saying there 
aren't any perk's to having static IP's, but from a management side 
there are a lot of perk's to DHCP as well.

- m0gely

On Fri, 2006-12-29 at 09:08 -0800, alan wrote:
> > [chaz> ]
> > I'm with Verizon. Looks like the Treo700p is what they offer.
> > Anyone have direct experience with it?

The 700p is the next generation; the resolution on the camera is much
better and there is more memory.  Aside from that and some packaging
differences, I don't think they're all that different.  Last time I
checked, though, VZW still offered the 650 and it was considerably less
than the 700.

> I have a couple of friends with one.  They look nice.  They run palm 
> software. I have heard that the 700p does not support the wireless sd 
> cards, but the 650 will. (For those who want to scan for wireless networks 
> with their phone. Tricorder sounds optional.)

This is actually just backwards--the 600 and 700 have Palm-supplied SDIO
WiFi options, but the 650 has only the option to use a 3rd party WiFi
adapter on the sync port (and it costs more too).

> They are not cheap, but no PDA phones are.

With contract and rebates, it's only about $200, which is expensive but
not nearly as expensive as the MSRP.

Wil

From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (someone)

References: <20061225201358.34078.qmail@web50613.mail.yahoo.com>
	<e2aeccd60612251719g7712b69cq9ed5a831e5319a87@mail.gmail.com>

	<1167165312.19758.47.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
	<4592FADD.5060003@schluting.com>
Message-ID: <1167330376.20472.10.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>


> If the domain in question isn't publishing SPF (chances are good.. most
> people know SPF is a horrible solution) then you don't have anything to
> worry about.

Now wait a minute Charlie, I'm not so sure SPF is a terrible solution.
It requires a new notion be followed that email for a particular domain
has to come from specific servers.  This is supposed to cut down on
allowing bot machines to flood the Internet with garbage.  How else can
you publish which servers on the Internet are serving your email?  If
I'm not mistaken, there is a special SPF enhancement for ISP sites that
forward other domain.

Now instead of saying, "SPF is a terrible solution...," how about 
posting a link to a web page explaining your opinion or giving your
reasons directly?

     -- Michael C. Robinson

Mike,

   I don't do tips -- except for wait staff in restaurants.

   But, here's a reference to the appropriate chapter in the good Postfix
book: <http://www.postfix-book.com/debugging.html>.

   Also, what do the logs say? Specifically, /var/log/maillog.

   I've had situations where a typo or other silly oopsie! caused a fatal
error that throttled the exec file. It's all in the log; not the source of
the problem, necessarily, but the overt results.

From mikeraz at patch.com  Fri Dec 29 16:58:13 2006
From: mikeraz at patch.com (Michael Rasmussen)
Date: Fri, 29 Dec 2006 16:58:13 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] postfix question
In-Reply-To: <200612291854.kBTIsALA014780@ruby.spiritone.com>
References: <200612291854.kBTIsALA014780@ruby.spiritone.com>
Message-ID: <20061230005813.GA2326@patch.com>

Mike Neal wrote:
> A fresh install of OpenSuse 10.2  running NAT, iptables, DNS and 
> postfix works great except mail is not being received, with or 
> without iptables rules.
> 
> Any tips appreciated.

Have you read through /etc/postfix/main.cf (assuming standard placement)?
The comments are pretty explanatory about what each option does and how to
configure it.

Specifically the comment:

  # The inet_interfaces parameter specifies the network interface
  # addresses that this mail system receives mail on.  By default,
  # the software claims all active interfaces on the machine. The
  # parameter also controls delivery of mail to user@[ip.address].
  #
  # See also the proxy_interfaces parameter, for network addresses that
  # are forwarded to us via a proxy or network address translator.
  #
  # Note: you need to stop/start Postfix when this parameter changes.
  #
  #inet_interfaces = all
  #inet_interfaces = $myhostname
  #inet_interfaces = $myhostname, localhost

would seem to indicate the source of your problem.

I would guess SUSE is being "helpful" (or you accepted a config for local mail
only) and restricting mail acceptance to localhost.

-- 
      Michael Rasmussen, Portland Oregon
