someone wrote:

> Dell Inspiron 5100 P4 2.8 Ghz...  I had to call Microsoft to install to
> vmware.  My girlfriend had to reinstall an OEM version of Windows XP 
> directly on her Celeron desktop and ended up having to call, she 
> didn't change any hardware.  Neither one of us was trying to install 
> to a different computer.

My comment was directed towards this "Microsoft is trying to restrict
legit use more than piracy".  If you're experience is much less 
tolerance from Windows for installed software or re-installation w/o any 
hardware changes, I'm not disputing that.  I just look at it as 5 to 10 
to minutes out of your day when it happened.  Does any other software or 
OS do this?  Well no for the most part but more are jumping on the 
"activation" bandwagon all the time.  And they're certainly not doing it 
to discourage legit users.  You're in a small minority. I would be 
surprised if MS hit 5% of a legit install base of Windows in some Asian 
countries.  This and casual copying is why WPA exists.

The desire to run commercial closed source software is no more wrong 
than a person who prefers open source, it's just different.  Both come 
with caveats.  I bet you've spent a lot more time hacking away at why 
some app didn't install/run or compile on your linux machine 100 fold 
more than WPA took out of your life, right?  I know this is the case 
with me and using FreeBSD, but I know this comes with the territory.

> How is Microsoft supposed to keep a call
> center staffed indefinitely for people to call in and reactivate 
> their copy of Windows?  If Microsoft is underpaying people in India,
> is this something any of us should be supporting?

As I said, Win9x is still supported with what updates are still 
available via Windows Update.  The same will be with XP.  You can 
speculate all you want, but it is years away when anything on this will 
be known either way.

> If you have a lot of custom software for NT4.0 that you don't want to
> port to something else, that can be a good enough reason to keep NT 4.0.

You can run NT4 for as long as you like.  Just like *any* OS which has 
reached the end of it's life cycle, you just need to take the proper 
precautions for where it's used, be it public or private in nature.  But 
eventually when the only hardware available never had drivers for NT4 
written for it, you'll port. :)

> There will come a time when you don't want to fight activation
> mOgerly.  A time will come when having to call someone will be 
> asking too much of you, especially if the call center ever shuts 
> down or you lose phone service and can't call out.

For now, activation is small on the list of things I don't like about 
MS.  Far more important to me are their business practices among other 
things.  Personally I see myself owning a Mac as my primary computer in 
the future, or getting to the point FreeBSD can be what I primarily use. 
  I'm tied to it because of a couple apps though.

> Why mogerly are you defending WPA by saying that it's not the 
> big deal as I'm making it out to be?

eh, it's "m0gely".  You know, like in the Disney flick, but l337? ;)

Because in my experience, it's not.  I know some people have had bad 
experiences with it, and for some more recently it has even involved 
re-installing the OS to make WPA happy.  This would suck.  I just know 
it's not true for the masses, and dispute your implication that MS seems 
to be targeting legit users more so than piracy.

> I've seen ReactOS bashed 

Eh, good luck to them, but they are not a realistic option in any 
regard.  They run a *very limited* set of actual Windows applications, 
none of which are productivity based.  It's just not even a 
consideration.  What does ReactOS have over Ubuntu?

On Thu, Dec 28, 2006 at 06:58:06PM -0800, m0gely wrote:
> Spiritone.com (happy customer, don't work there)

I maintain an offsite virtual colo for $21 a month, with two static
IP addresses.  I manage 13 domains and all their email through that.
Besides having mondo bandwidth (10GB/sec) to the facility in Dallas,
if my home office goes down the mail gets spooled on the colo until
it comes back up. 

The virtual machine is a Xen image, so if the Dallas site goes down
it (and it would probably take a nuclear weapon to do so) then the
image migrates to London or Brisbane.  I reach it through an
outbound OpenVPN link from my firewall (on Comcast), which has no
incoming services.   Because my DNS is at Dyndns, I can change colo
providers in minutes, and because I have no inbound services I can
change ISPs quickly, too.  The only real weak point is Dyndns, and
at least the name service is cached if they go offline.

I use rimuhosting for the virtual colo because they provide prompt
and outstanding 24x7 service.  I hope to return to a local company
someday, if one of them develops similar stability and service; 
perhaps if the small companies banded together into a mutual support
network, I could get the same features I get from rimuhosting.

Keith

On the topic of email I heard through the grapevine that Gmail is going to
offer the ability to get email from any account from within the Gmail
interface in the near future so that could resolve all your problems right
there.

<tangent about DHCP versus static>


I've worked for several ISP's and currently a telecom provider and  static
is just not feasible in the long run if you want to scale to anything larger
then a small mom and pop operation (not that there is anything wrong with
that).

>From a managment standpoint DHCP is much easier but requires more upfront
work (DHCP server, Accounting/Radius). If you're using a static setup you
risk having only roughly 63 customers per /24 subnet (depending on how its
engineered) so you're looking at additional cost as you grow because you're
paying someone to submit applications to ARIN for new subnets as well as
paying the registration fee's. Of course if you go DHCP then you have to
deal with things like the MPAA and RIAA complaints and being able to
accurately correlate authentication and IP data to a specific account which
takes time and money :-)

This idea could be argued for ever and ever but I guess comes down to the
time and money and engineering you want to put into a network

</tangent>

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Fri Dec 29 13:12:47 2006
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (someone)
Date: Fri, 29 Dec 2006 13:12:47 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] E-mail server?
In-Reply-To: <4594843E.1000103@telestream.com>
References: <20061225201358.34078.qmail@web50613.mail.yahoo.com>
	<e2aeccd60612251719g7712b69cq9ed5a831e5319a87@mail.gmail.com>
	<4590A6C9.9070307@telestream.com>
	<1167165312.19758.47.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
	<4594843E.1000103@telestream.com>
Message-ID: <1167426767.25615.53.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>


> All I can say is if you really think about it, and think about the 
> future, dynamic IP's are a nice thing to have.  I'm not saying there 
> aren't any perk's to having static IP's, but from a management side 
> there are a lot of perk's to DHCP as well.

You can do static ip addresses with DHCP.  I do this on my own private
network, no problem.  It means that I don't have to do dns updates,
I can keep my dns servers static.  How fast can Internet based dns
servers update for dynamic ip addresses?  Seems you are going to be
stuck having someone on a static address act as a middleman.  The only
downside of static ip addresses via dhcp is that you need your
customer's MAC address and a hostname.  Unless you are only a client 
and not a server, I don't see how dynamic ip addresses are ever a 
good idea.

Sure it's less work to randomly hand out ip addresses.  It's not a whole
lot less though.  Static assignments aren't a lot of work if you have a
web interface that the customer can access to inform you of: the
hostname they want, the ip address they want it to use, and the reverse
hostname that they want.  You should be able to know which physical
segment a customer is coming in on and if they specify the correct dhcp
hostname, give them the corresponding ip address.  Figure out how to set
this up securely and automate it fully, static assignment through dhcp
ends up being as easy as dynamic assignment.

Why is dynamically assigning ip addresses better than staticly 
assigning them?  What is the advantage of putting customers on
random global ip addresses within a single subnet, or less than
that?  As far as anonymizing people is concerned, having a 
different ip address every time you browse the Internet isn't 
much protection.  Think about it, if someone wants to harass
you and you are 1 out of say 255 ip addresses, that isn't very
many possibilities if they can spy on all them.  Then once they
notice the habits of one of those ip addresses matches yours,
they might be able to port scan the ip address and fire off an
attack.  How often do dynamic ip addresses change?  With Comcast,
you can easily go for a few months with the same number.  

Changing one's ip address frequently doesn't address problems 
with confidential information leaking out of databases on the 
net.  Worse, what if your ip address times out in the middle 
of a large download?  For ip based security, a moving target
weakens those schemes.  I allow a specific ip to come in and
access services, the next minute it doesn't belong to the right
computer anymore.

I can't get lightspeed research for example to leave me alone.
Maybe when I trusted them giving them email information and
possibly more than that, I made a huge mistake.  Right now, there
doesn't seem to be any way to clean up mistakes.  There are no 
Internet cops that can receive an empty me from the database 
order then both investigate and enforce it.  A similar problem, 
being on the government's do not call list is not working for a 
lot of people.  If I do a credit card transaction with company 
X over the Net, I should be able to order that my information 
be removed from their database once the transaction is complete.
What are you supposed to do, use a credit card once on the 
Internet and then cancel it?  Frequent cancellations of credit
cards will probably wreck your credit rating.  

What makes me really angry is when a company like Opus 
Interactive double charges a customer's credit card.  They 
insist on having it on file and won't take checks anymore, 
yet supposedly the DSL service they offer is month to month 
and not contract based.  If there is no contract, why on 
earth does Opus have to have your credit card on file?

<Aside>
Whether it's http://www.ccleaner.com/ or some other site does
it tick anyone off that there are often these you have won something
ads and you can't claim it without making a purchase?  These ads to
me seem like illegal schemes, yet they appear to be rampant across
the Internet.  The lightspeed research outfit gives you this do surveys,
earn points, get stuff pitch.  It's a lie, you never have enough points.
In the meantime, you might mistakenly trust a BBB icon and a Verisign
symbol and foolishly hand them your soc number.
</Aside>

Michael C. Robinson



From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Fri Dec 29 14:59:11 2006
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (someone)
Date: Fri, 29 Dec 2006 14:59:11 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Phone/PDA Recommendations
In-Reply-To: <1167416238.6848.7.camel@willow.odshp.com>
References: <B0028771870@bct-mail.bctonline.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0612290905490.7832@blackbox.fnordora.org>
	<1167416238.6848.7.camel@willow.odshp.com>

Why combine a cell phone and a palmtop?  As far as video
telephony goes, most people don't want to pay for broadband
on a portable phone.  Phones are pretty simple devices, why
complicate that?  OSS software seems to have been slow to 
penetrate the palmtop/PDA market.  Are there any efforts 
to design a palmtop or PDA specifically for Linux users?

A PDA that is wifi and Linux ready makes a lot of sense.  I don't
understand all the hype about bluetooth.  My impression is that 
it is a hobbled wireless technology.  IR is badly hobbled these 
days also.  

IR was hobbled supposedly because students were cheating on tests 
by communicating with someone remotely using the IR feature of their
calculators.  I have no idea why you can't change the design of
classrooms to deal with IR abuse or simply flunk cheaters instead 
of ruining a superb technology.

There seems to be a lot of resistance to developing and selling OSS
controlled devices in the US.  I have a hard time accepting that our
laws are strongly against doing so.  It seems that few US companies 
see the economy of scale that can be achieved if they design popular
consumer electronic devices which are 100% Linux compatible in the first
place.  You shift from making money by having a niche through a trade
secret to bulk producing a quality product that people want and can use
with OSS software.

With palmtops and PDAs, you have a small screen to work on and usually
no keyboard.  These problems aren't easily solved unless someone
perfects holographic projection.  You can't make the picture
bigger by attaching a huge LCD monitor.  Does it make sense to pay
for and have the equivalent of a high end computer in a palm top
form factor?  I don't think it does.  With the restrictions, it makes
more sense for the machine to be a personal organizer and scientific
calculator than a full blown computer.  Show me a $100 palm top that
can: be used to connect to evolution and ical on a full sized computer,
be used as a scientific calculator, be used to scribble notes on, and be
used to keep contact information handy.  And oh yeah, I want a credit
card slot on the side of it so I can keep track of my credit and debit
card balances by simply swiping them when they are used and entering
the amount that has been withdrawn or deposited.  It would be nice to
tweak checkbook tracker for this.  

     Michael C. Robinson

> Eh, good luck to them, but they are not a realistic option in any 
> regard.  They run a *very limited* set of actual Windows applications, 
> none of which are productivity based.  It's just not even a 
> consideration.  What does ReactOS have over Ubuntu?

Ubuntu uses proprietary enhancements that are likely to disappear
or otherwise not be carried forward into the next release.  ReactOS
doesn't use any proprietary code.

What productivity application that require Windows anything are you
referring to?  

For 20 programmers, ReactOS is doing well supporting some Windows
software.  Instead of saying it isn't an option, why don't you go to
http://www.reactos.org/ and join the development effort?  There is
plenty of participation for Linux to develop further.  Someone tries to
make a GPL'ed OS that is 100% compatible with Windows XP and 2000, there
is practically none.  Why is that?  There is potential, plenty of it.
2009 for a stable release time line is worrisome, but it's
understandable with only 20 programmers that it will take that long.  XP
came out in 2002, so that's only 7 years to come out with an
alternative.  Maybe more people will care about ReactOS when it gets
further along, but that is just the problem with projects.  They often
don't get off the ground because people despair when their support is
needed most.

Porting of software to Linux and FreeBSD from Windows isn't something
that is happening very often.  ReactOS is a project that is an effort 
to work around this problem.  I think they are doing pretty well
considering that all 20 of these programmers have to support themselves
via work outside the ReactOS project.

How many years went into developing XP?  At least 2 years went into it
and probably 4+.  Granted, ReactOS has been developing for 10 years.
How long does reverse engineering take when you are trying to make 
sure that you are doing so legally and you only have a limited number 
of programmers?  Never mind that there are limited resources.

The idea of a an OSS OS that you can go to from Windows where you won't
have to retrain makes a lot of sense.  With a future plan to give
ReactOS the ability to run Linux software, people can migrate from
ReactOS over.  ReactOS is trying to do one thing Microsoft has
abandoned, make the NT platform efficient.  Microsoft doesn't keep it's
application and OS people from unduly influencing each other.  There are
apparently going to be a ton of inconsistencies in Vista.  Vista has a
virgin network stack and probably more fresh code.  Security wise, this
is a disaster.  Microsoft is saying that Vista is the most secure system
they have ever developed already.  I bet the day of the official release
that someone will crack it.

   Michael C. Robinson
